Definition .   Zeroing is the testing and, if nec, a djusting the sights so that at any rg the  line of sight and the line of bore coincide at one pt.  2.   Occasions .   The gun is zeroed:   -  a.   On receipt from Ord dep. Before firing, annual rg cl.  b.   On replacement or repair of maj part.  c.   On replacement of the original   barrel.  d.   Before going into battle.

--- PAGE 80 ---
80  RESTD  RESTD  e.   When the gun nos are changed.  f.   When the unit   mov from one sta to another.  3.   Conditions Req for Zeroing .   Fol conditions must be kept in view before carrying  out zeroing: -  a.   Rg should be 100 ms.  b.   Rg on the back sight should be   200 ms.  c.   MPI should be 7.62 cm (3 in) above the pt of aim.  d.   No 1 should zero the gun and other no should test the zeroing of the gun.  e.   Sel a clear and calm day when no wind is blowing.  f.   Blacken the sights.  4.   Methods of Zeroing .   There are two methods: -  a.   “Gp me thods” by firing a gp of 4 to 5 rds.  b.   “Burst methods”, by firing a burst of 10 rds (in MG only).  5.   Principles of Zeroing  a.   For vertical adjustment mov out of the error.  b.   For lateral adjustment mov into the error.  c.   Fire a check gp.  6.   Methods of Adjustment .   Wha tever the method of zeroing, after determining the mean  pt of impact (MPI) of the bullets. Proceed as under:   -  a.   Determine the vertical and lateral errors from the pt  of aim.  b.   Prior to carrying   out adjustment, ensure that   yellow  index mk on t he elev screw   coincides with   yellow  mk   provided on the lt side of   body of the rg scale  plate. From this posn of the elev screw, 5 clicks  down are eff. Eliminate vertical error by adjusting  the elev screw. Inc or dec of 1 click will make a diff  of 1 in at 100 ms (109.33   yds).   Total adjustment is of 25.4 cm .  c.   Eliminate lateral error by loosening the  front sight adjusting screw and shifting  front   side   blade   as   req.   Shifting   the  foresight to either side by one thickness of  the blade will make a diff of 18 cms (7 in  approx)   at   100   m   (109.33   yds).   Total  adjustment is of 50.8 cms (20 in).  BORE SIGHTING  Definition  1.   Bore sighting is the testing and, if nec, adjusting the sights so that at a fixed rg the line of  sight and the line of bore coincide at one pt.  Occasions  2.   Bore s ighting of gun can be carried out: -  a.   Before firing annual rg cl.  b.   On replacement or repair of front sight or back sight.  c.   During bad weather.  d.   Due to non aval of firing rgs.  Note :   Inc or dec of 100 m (109.33 yds) on the back sight will make a diff of   15 cm (6 in) at 200 m (218.75  yds).

--- PAGE 81 ---
81  RESTD  RESTD  Advs  3.   Some of the advs of bore sighting are as fol: -  a.   With the help of bore sight ing, zeroing of the wpns is achieved in less time, i.e.  coinciding the line of sight and the line of bore on the pt of aim.  b.   Reduces the expenditures of ammo during zeroing.  c.   Bore sighting can easily be carried out in Bk /indoor during bad weather.  d.   Whene ver the firer feels during the firing that this wpn is not firing accurately, he  can very conveniently check the bore sighting of his wpn in the shortest possible  time.  e.   Confidence of the weak firers can also be revived on their wpns as they can also  zer o their wpns accurately by this method.  f.   This method can be prac during the battle also, where normal zeroing is not  practicable.  Methods of Bore Sighting  4.   There are two methods of bore sighting: -  a.   Testing tgt method.  b.   Distant tgt method.  Stores  5.   Before   we go over to the methods of bore sighting of MG1A3, let’s see what all stores are  req for bore sighting in both the methods: -  a.   LMG.  b.   Aiming Rest.  c.   Black Thread.  d.   Testing Tgt.  e.   Stand for Black B d for placing the testing tgt (stand used for the bore sightin g of  RL be used).  f.   Plumb Line.  g.   L type keys/screw d vr.  h.   Hammer small.  i.   Pencil (For marking the posn of fore sight blade).  j.   Black B ds and chalks.  k.   Grease (For sticking the thread).  l.   Clinometer (held by 60 mm Mor det can be used).  6.   Testing Tgt Method  a.   Prep o f the gun: -  (1)   Take out the bolt and carrier gp and strip in a manner that you are lt with  the bolt head.  (2)   Place the gun on the aiming rest and make it parallel to the gr with the  help of Clinometer.  (3)   Place the bolt head in the gun in a way that its rear end   touches the  terminating walls of the receiver slide, towards the butt End.  (4)   Remove the recoil booster and make thread cross on the barrel guide  sleeves, sticking the threads at four ends with the grease. Make sure that  intersection of threads   gives   you th e exact line of bore or the cen of bore  and the cross is upright.

--- PAGE 82 ---
82  RESTD  RESTD  Sight Cross  Bore Cross  (5)   The firing pin way in the bolt will give you the line of bore, performing the  job of a rear bore disc.  (6)   Set 200 ms rg at the rear sight of the gun.  b.   Place the testing tgt at a dist of 10 f t from the front end of the barrel guide sleeve.  An error of 1 inch is allowed.  c.   No looking through the firing pin hole  coincide   bore   sight   cross   with   the  bottom cross of the testing tgt. Seeing  through bore sight has to be done from  the same dist as would   a firer do during  the firing. Removal of butt assy reduces  the dist by about 5 - 6 inches so firer  should keep his f ace 5 - 6 inches away  from gun.  d.   Then looking through sights sees that the  line   of   sight   is   coinciding   with   the  intersection of the upper cros s.  e.   If the line of sight is coinciding, bore  sighting of your wpn is correct. If it is not  coinciding with the intersection of the  upper cross laterally or vertically, remove  the error by adjusting front sight tip and  elev adjusting screw, in a manner tha t your  line   of   sight   coincides   with   the  intersection   of   the   upper   cross   on  testing tgt.  f.   Check again, aligning the bore sighting cross  and line of sight with respect ive crosses  on the testing tgt.  7.   Distant Tgt Method  a.   Sel a conspicuous pt at a dist of 100 ms . Preferable pt at the top or having one  plain side for ease of coinciding the bore sighting cross.  b.   Prep the gun as already explained in sub Para 14a and ensure that 200 ms rg is  applied on the rear sight.  c.   Now looking through the firing pin hole, coincid e the bore sight cross with the pt  sel.  d.   Then check that the top of fore sight   coincides   on the same pt on the gr. If this is  so, your wpn is correctly bore sighted. However, in case of any variation make the  nec adjustment in front sight tip and rear sigh t.  e.   After making desired adjustments, once again check that line of bore and line of  sight are coinciding at the same pt.

By showing the rgs and dirs of  nearby objs, it enables the gun crew to determine quickly and accur ately the data nec to engage  any tgt in the sec. Usually secs of fire are up to 180 degs or less. If the occasion demands, the rg  card is prep to incl any tgt within 360   degs all around the gun posn.  2.   Rg card makes it possible to fire at ni and duri ng pds   of ltd vis and to tfr ava l info to pers  of a relieving unit. The next higher fmn sometimes uses these rg cards to coord arcs of fire. The  rg card is always made in two copies. One is sent to the next higher comd. Rg cards are prep for  the original and the   altn posns of the gun. Mil symbols are used in their prep.  Prep of Rg Card  3.   The rg cards are prep as under: -  a.   Write the unit designation and data in one corner of a piece of paper of suitable  size.  b.   Place a dot in the approx   cen of the paper to denote th e gun posn.  c.   Draw a straight line through the   gun posn to represent magnetic n orth.  d.   Draw line representing the gen dir of fire.  e.   Considering the gun posn as cen, draw diff circles to indicate the req rgs i.e. 400,  800,   1200,   1600   and   2000   ms   (437.33,   8751 312,   1750,   and   2187   yds  respectively). The gun should be pt in the dir of the cen of the arc of fire. Measure  angle of deflection (traversing bar reading) to probable tgts and fixed lin e, (if  used from the zero line).  f.   When a fix ed line, as near to fwd def   loc as possible, is used, determine and record  the magnetic bearing deflection reading, elev mech reading and rg to the limit of  graze and to the near and far ends of any gaps (dead space) along the fixed line.  g.   Indicate the rt or lt limit of the arc by   drawing a   dotted line   from the gun posn.  h.   If friendly tps are within or adjacent to the arc of fire, indicate their locs.  i.   Draw straight lines from gun posn to probable tgts.  j.   Determine the rg, elev mech reading and deflection for each tgt. Sketch the tgts   on  proper lines and mk them.  k.   Record the rg, elev mech reading and deflection on each line.

The low lt that is aval at ni enters the sight through obj lens.  b.   The lenses of the obj lens assy have anti - reflective coatings so that the incoming lt  will not be reflected, making the detection of the sight op practically   impossible.  c.   The obj lens inverts the image from the observed tgt, and focuses it onto the  photo - cathode of the image intensifier tube.  d.   The focused image is intensified 20,000   –   40,000 times, inverted again and proj to  the phosphor screen at the tube opt ical output.  e.   The image intensifying tube is autogated, therefore it reg the gain according to  the external lighting cond itions .

FLK FIRE  (GSP - 1420, 7.63 mm MACHINE GUN, MG1A3, 1970)  1.   Apch .   Provn of   sp ing fire to own tps is the main   tactical role of the MG s. The safety of  the tps, to whom such sp i s being given, must be the 1 st   cons d of the   comd .  2.   Flk Fire .   Fire sp   can be provided either from a flk or over the head of own tps.  a.   Whenever possible, sp should be provided from posns on the flk. This has the fol  advs: -  (1)   Greater fire eff is gen obtained from the beate n zone in enfilade or flk fire.  (2)   Fire from a flk can be   put down with considerable safety closer to the tps  being sp, tha n in the case of overhead fire.  b.   The guns can be brought into action quicker for flk fire than for overhead fire. In  overhead fire, considerable time is req to determ ine and calc the safety li mits.

--- PAGE 95 ---
95  RESTD  RESTD  c.   Flk fire can be provided under timed progm. In the case of overhead fire,  however, it can be done safely only if the tps being sp, r igidly stick to the mov  progm.  3.   Rules of Flk Fire .   Rules governing the provn of flk fire are: -  a.   In Def, the fixed l ine must not be laid closer than 60 mils (3 deg) from the fwd  edge of the FDLs.  b.   In Attk, fire on the obj must be stopped as soon as own tps reach a line 3 deg from  the obj.  c.   The 3 deg limit line must be extn to a pt 500 m (546 - 566 yds) beyond the pt of  a im. In other words, own tps should not be within 500 m (545 - 566 yds) in the  line of the aiming pt (the highest elev used) when giving flk fire sp.  d.   This type of fire must not be attempted if posn of own tps in the vicinity of the  danger area is not known.   As already pt out, this entails either obsn of own tps or  timed progm based on their rate of adv, which must not be exceeded by the tps  concerned. In def, own line of FDLs must be known.  e.   Arngs should be made to prevent traver sing inside 3 deg safety limit .   Allce must  be made for side winds, for e.g: -  (1)   In Def, while giving fire as near as possible to own tps if wind were  blowing towards own tps an allce of 5 mils would be nec to place the  safety angle, 5 mils farther away or in other words inc the safety al lce to  65 mils. On the other hand, if the wind is blowing away from FDLs no allce  would be made.  (2)   In Attk, if the wind was blowing in the dir of adv of own tps, inc the safety  allce. If the wind was blowing along the dir of adv, incl it in the fire orders  for laying the gun but excl it from the safety angle.  f.   Gun and tripod must be in good condition.  4.   Consds in Flk Fire  a.   During trg, always give 120 mils (6 deg) safety allce.  b.   Never measure the safety angle with the hand.  c.   In Def, full liaison with own tps sh ould be maint, so that any changes in own  disposns are known.  d.   Gun should be properly stamped in. Use sandbags.  5.   Safety Angle .   The lateral safety allce of 60 mils (3 deg) is given to cover: -  a.   Minor inaccuracy in laying.  b.   Mov of mount settling in during the   initial bursts of fire.  c.   Half the width of the beaten zone.  d.   Allce for normal side wind.  6.   Safety Limits .   Safety limit is the 60 mils (3 deg), plus allce for wind if any, b/w own tps  and the line of fire within which own tps are not considered safe. This s afety limit can be on the  lt or the rt depending upon the sit.  7.   This limit should preferably be at 3 or 9’o clock from the tgt. If this cannot be helped, sel  it at 2, 4, 8 or 10’o clock. Care will be ex to measure 60 mils (3 deg) from the line of either 3’o  clock or 9’o clock only.  Fire and Mov  8.   The sec method of giving sp to the attk tps is by fire and mov. The fol rules must be fol: -  a.   Guns should mov bound by bound.

The Leopard Crawl  (1)   The leopard crawl is very much the same as with a rif.  (2)   Hold the small of the butt with yourright hand, the folded bipod with your  left and keep the pistol grip rearwards. Do not forget to make the LMG  safe before crawling it .  b.   The Side Crawl  (1)   Lie on either side, hold the gun by it’s carrying handle or pt of bal with  your upper hand and rest the butt on your lower leg.  (2)   Use your upper foot and lower elbow to push you along.  c.   The Knee Crawl  (1)   Use the knee crawl behind waist high cove r.  (2)   Kneel on your right knee, with your right fist flat on the gr. Hold the gun  with your left hand by the carrying handle, or from the pt of balance. In  the former case, rest your thumb on your knee to take some of the weight  of the gun off your arms.  (3)   To a dvance, move your left foot and right fist forward together and bring  your right knee upto them and so on.  d.   The Walk  (1)   During assault by day or by night and during periods of reduced visibility  or wooded country, an LMG firer may have to walk towards the enem y.  There may be situations when a heavy vol of fire is desired in the tgt area  and rapid movement is essential. In such fast - moving assault situations, to  facilitate carrying of LMG in ready to fire posn for extended periods of  time single or a pair of sli ngs joined together should be used. This will  provide addl sp to the wpn in   firing from the hip or under arm posns.  (2)   The method of holding the LMG and firing with it on the move is the same  as with the rif. The gunner must lean well fwd towards the tgt befo re and  during firing to attain stability.  (3)   A short burst should be fired each time the left foot strikes the gr and fire  should be adjusted by observing the tracers or impact of the bullets in the  tgt area.

The Combined Leopard Crawl by bounds .   It is the same as the ordinary  combined leopard crawl, except that the men crawl forward as far as they can  without letting go of the gun and then lift the gun as far fo rward as they can, and  so on. A short duration, high  power pulse from IR laser is emitted from the transmitter when the trigger button is pressed.  The ctr starts counting and as soon as the scatt ered lt or echo from the tgt is picked up by the obj  Figure   10   Principle of Op  To target  echo  Start Pulse  RECEIVER  Transmitter  Reticle  Sighting Eye Piece  Eye Protection Filter  Display Eye Piece  Display  Electronics  Detector

Onc e the LRF is switched on and the trigger button is pressed within a sec  or so, a green indication lamp on the upper part of the fd of view in the display eyepiece lights  up which show that the circuits in LRF are  ready for rg. When the trigger button is re l  after   taking   aim   of   the   tgt,   the   laser  transmitter emits a laser pulse. This laser  pulse bifurcates into two. The maj portion  of the pulse is dir towards the tgt. The  other portion of pulse is fed to the sys to  start the counter. In the meantime, the  las er pulse transmitted towards the tgt is  reflected back, which is called laser echo  pulse, and enters into the rg finder (Fig  11). The counter stops counting. The elec  sys translates the time interval datum in  the appearing b/w two digits should not be take n as a decimal pt. When objs exist within the  Min Rg Gate or beyond the tgt to be measured, the logic module can ident various echo sigs  conveyed from the receiver, which are indicated on the display unit in the form of dots,  indicating multiple tgts. 112  RESTD  RESTD  GEN DESCRIPTION AND HANDLING OF 9MM PISTOLS  Research paper  Intro  1.   The pistol is used to kill and wound indl / live tgt within 50m. These pistols fire DAP92  9mm ball carts and 9 x 19 parabellum pistol carts.  Structural Principal and Features  2.   The   firing mode of the pistol is semi - auto. The locking mech is barrel - rotating type. The  method of firing is hammer type and firing mech is pull rod disengaging type. It can conduct  single action and joint action firing. In addn to a manual safety, the pistol   is also provided with a  firing pin safety and func of cart - in - bore indication, ammo res indication and bolt catch.  Furthermore, a simple mech sight and a   luminous   ni aiming pt are also arng. A laser sight can be  set when nec.  Op  3.   The op process of pistol   is discussed below;   -  a.   Insert the mag containing carts into the mag slot of the grip till the mag is caught.  Check mag lever for resetting after inserting.  b.   Pull the bolt backward and release it after it mov to posn. Under the action of  return spring, the   pistol is in cocked posn and can be fire by pulling trigger.  c.   After firing, the bolt mov backward to complete cart case extraction and ejection,  press the hammer down and repeat the return and loading.  d.   The next rd of cart can be fired by pulling the trigg er till bolt stop state occurs. At  this time, the mag can be replaced by pressing the mag lever.  e.   If it is needed to cont firing, press the hang - up lever (also can pull the bolt  backward to posn and then rel it) to return the bolt to posn.  f.   To deal with s udden case, safety with carts can be used in spec sit. To fire, open  the safety with a thumb to implement the first rd firing in joint action mode.  g.   In case of failure to fire, implement supplementary firing in joint action mode.  Accidental fire will not o ccur due to fall or other reason.  h.   Manual Safety .   When the pistol is at cocked posn, push the safety upward  with the thumb to horizontal safety posn, let the hammer rotates to safety posn.  To open the safety, press the safety with the thumb and rotate to f iring posn, the  hammer is set free. At this time, joint action firing can be implemented.   (Caution:  when the hammer is at firing posn, the safety cannot be turned. To close the  safety, first press the hammer backward to cocked posn and the turn the  safety)  i.   Cart - in - bore Indication .   If there were any carts in bore, the indication lever at  the front of the extractor would protrude out of the extractor surface, on the  contrary, the indication lever would fall from the extractor surface.  j.   Ammo Res Indication .   Take out the mag, residual carts can be dir read out  from the no engraved on the sq slot of the mag.  Maint and Safety Precautions  4.   Maint .   After firing, fol maint is req.  a.   Check the chamber for cart.  b.   Clean and coat the mov components with oil in time   to prevent from corrosion.  c.   Troubles occurring in the course of firing must be solved in time. Remove the  misfired cart from the chamber and dispose of properly.

116  RESTD  RESTD  BASIC PISTOL SHOOTING TECH  Research paper (www.lawenforcementservices.biz)  Intro  1.   Accurate, quick and eff pistol shooting is an expertise and can only be achieved by  exhaustive and comprehensiv e trg. Basic pistol shooting tech would serve as a beginning for  correct and eff firing.  2.   Normal Safety Precautions (NSP) .   Safe wpn handling begins with four steps  whenever you touch a wpn:   -  a.   Handle all wpns as if they are loaded.  b.   Keep the wpn pointed   in a safe dir.  c.   Keep you finger out of the trigger gd.  d.   Imed check to make certain the wpn is unloaded. Clear the wpn if nec.  3.   The Mksmanship Principles .   The mksmanship principles are as under:   -  a.   Posn and hold should be firm enough to sp the wpn.  b.   Wpn sh ould pt naturally at the tgt.  c.   Sight alignment / aiming must be correct.  d.   Each shot released and fol through.  4.   Fundamentals of Pistol Shooting  a.   Correct stance.  b.   Firm grip.  c.   Alignment of front and rear sight.  d.   Trigger con.  e.   Area where you want to fire.  f.   Con   breathing.  g.   Fol through.  5.   Stances  a.   Isosceles stance . An isosceles triangle has three equal sides. The isosceles posn  is accomplished by facing the tgt with ft spaced a little wider than shoulder width  apart. The legs are slightly bent, the wt is on the ft , the back is straight, and the  head is erect. The wpn is brought up - to - the - eye. Do not hunch fwd with the head  and shoulders. The arms are straight and the arms and body form an isosceles  triangle when viewed from above. Total body posture during isoscele s simply  means that the ft, hips, backbone, shoulders, neck, and head are all aligned in  relationship to each other.

--- PAGE 117 ---
117  RESTD  RESTD  b.   Weaver stance . This posn is named after a police officer. The weaver   is  accomplished by standing with the ft basically par allel to each other; however,  both ft are angled 45 deg from the tgt. Most indls do not understand this basic  posn. Instead, they simply move the rt ft back and angle it at 45 degrees. With the  lt ft pt forward and the rt ft angled at 45 deg. Total body po sture again means that  the ft, hips, backbone, and shoulders are all aligned. A common mistake is to twist  the hips, backbone, or shoulders and thus a natural pt of aim is lost. The head is  the only portion of the body that is turned to face the tgt. Durin g the weaver posn,  the rt arm is straight and the lt arm is bent 45 degrees to allow the wpn to align  with the head.   Another common mistake when utilizing the weaver posn is to  bent the head to the rt . The head should remain erect as the sights are brought  up to the head, not the head canted to the sights  6.   Stance Sel  a.   Stance must provide stability for both shooter and wpn.  b.   Stance should enable the firer to pt there pistol dir at the tgt.  c.   It confines vertical & horizontal mov / aim area.  7.   Stan ce Sel  a.   Indls may combine some char peculiar to them.  b.   You should select a stance as per your ht, wt and str.  c.   No definite all purpose stance.  d.   One should find his stance that produces best results.  8.   Stance Req  a.   Stance should enable the body posn which s p the pistol while aiming at the tgt.  b.   It strives for equilibrium, stability with least muscle strain.  c.   Head should be erected with both the eyes open acquiring the tgt.  9.   Grip .   Proper   grip   is   very   imp   when   shooting   a  pistol. When a pistol is fired the   wpn begins to recoil before  the bullet has left the barrel. Therefore, it is essential that the  grip   remain   consistent,   shot - after - shot.   Often   shooters  readjust their grip after each shot. The first shot will seat the  wpn firmly into the hand. Re - adj the g rip will loosen and  change the grip. Grasp the wpn with the web of the hand  pressed firmly into the back strap of the grip and once the  first shot is fired, do not readj the grip.

--- PAGE 118 ---
118  RESTD  RESTD  10.   The shooting hand should provide approximately 30%  of the grip with the sp   hand providing the remaining 70%.  This is nec so that the trigger finger will mov indep of the  other fingers. In other words, the shooting hand should not  clutch the wpn every time the trigger is pulled.   When  gripping the wpn, cover as much of the grips   of the wpn as  possible, filling as many gaps as possible. The wrist of the sp  hand is angled slightly fwd so that the thumb of the sp hand is  pt toward the tgt. If opened the fingers of the sp hand would  be angled at a 45º angle toward the gr. Utilizing th is posn, the  thumb of the sp hand will be ahead of the str hand thumb by  approx one inch. The natural curvature of the thumbs should  align symmetrically. The thumbs should not be interlocked.  Furthermore, the thumbs should not interfere with the op of  the   trigger, the safety, the slide latch, the mov of the slide, or  the mag release.  11.   Do not place the index finger of the sp hand on the front of the trigger gd. Many fire arms  have a serrated area on the front of the trigger gd. This modification is for stabi lity when firing  from a posn of cover and sp the wpn such as around the corner of a building of the hood of a car.  It is not designed for finger placement. If the index finger of the sp hand is placed on this  serrated area, the finger usually slips off dur ing firing of magnum rds, thus a large percentage of  the grip from the sp hand is lost. Remember, the sp hand must provide 70% of the grip. Fol must  be adhered in maint pistol grip.  a.   Max sp for stability and con.  b.   The sight should be aligned with the grip.  c.   Good trigger con.  d.   Trigger finger posn correctly with pull pressure dir to the rear.  e.   Trigger finger op indep from the hand.  f.   Grip should be comfortable.  g.   Dry prac is very essential.  12.   Grip Alignment  13.   Checking Grip Alignment .   Check alig nment as fol: -  a.   Close eyes, raise and lower arm slightly.  b.   Open eyes and observe.  c.   If deviated, reposn grip and repeat.  BAD   GOOD

--- PAGE 119 ---
119  RESTD  RESTD  d.   Constant check of the alignment.  e.   When grip released then repeat.  14.   Grip Pressure  a.   Three finger pressures into palm not finger tips.  b.   Thumb rests on the frame providing sp but not pressures. Enough pressure to sp  during recoil & maint sight alignment consistently.  c.   Pressure needs to be uniform throughout the shooting cycle.  d.   Grip pressure greater than trigger pressure allowing trigger   finger to operate  independently.  e.   Avoid shaking of your hand.  f.   Tighter the grip, then relax slightly.  15.   Conditioning and Str  a.   Hand fatigue can quickly occur.  b.   Strong firing hand will help.  c.   Build up strength exs.  d.   Greater str inc threshold for shake allow ing better grip.  16.   Sight Alignment  a.   It should show full sight picture.  b.   Remember (Equal ht, equal lt).  c.   Sight misalignment causes great error at tgt.  d.   Sight alignment must be maint for every shot.  e.   Focus fully on the fore sight.  f.   Sight alignment star ts with the grip.  g.   Accept you will get mov (aim area).  h.   Do not delay and wait for the perfect shot, it will not happen.

--- PAGE 120 ---
120  RESTD  RESTD  17.   Trigger Con  a.   Constant squeeze of the trigger w/o disturbing the sights.  b.   Good grip allows correct trigger fin ger placement and good trigger con.  c.   Put pressure to straight the rear.  d.   Dry trg is imp.  e.   Max pressure delay on the trigger should be within 6 sec.  f.   Avoid staged trigger op.  g.   During recoil take up the slack.  h.   This cycle starts again before pistol settles.  i.   Always look at the fore sight not at the trigger.  18.   Aim Area  a.   The sights are fixed do not req any adjustment.  b.   Fires must allow for centre of mass.  c.   Always think of aiming off.  19.   Fol Through  a.   Try to avoid disturbing the sight picture until the rd has left   the barrel.  b.   During fol through reset and re - apply trigger pressure.  c.   Always realign your sights as if for another shot.  20.   Breathing Con  a.   It must be uniform and systematic.  b.   Mov of chest, abdomen and shoulders results in poor shot.  c.   Plan a con breathing fo r each shot.  d.   A slow shot can be fired during normal breathing cycle.  e.   You can pause the normal breathing to rel the shot.  f.   It often helps to take a deep breath prior to a shot.  g.   Correct breathing con is essential to your con shot.  h.   Always prac breathe   con in dry prac.  i.   Breath must be sub - conscious.  21.   Fol Through  a.   Bad habits in this basic stage will affect rapid fire rhythm later.  b.   Fol through and recovery will highlt all the fundamentals (wrong or right).

--- PAGE 122 ---
122  RESTD  RESTD  FUNDAMENTALS   OF MILITARY INSTRUCTION  1.   Introduction .   The fundamental elements of an instructional situation are: -  a.   The student.  b.   The instructor.  c.   The teaching process, which consists of the five stages of instruction:  (1)   Preparation.  (2)   Presentation.  (3)   Application.  (4)   Examination.  (5)   Re view and/or critique.  Section I   -   THE STUDENT  2.   The Instructor Must Know His Students .   To   be   successful,   instructors   must  understand their students and the way they learn. As far as is possible, instructors should know  their men as individuals, appreciate t heir learning problems, and make every effort to help each  man learn. Instructors must see the course of instruction from the point of view of the learner  and plan accordingly.  3.   Characteristics Common to Most Students .   Most   military   students,   however,  have   the following common characteristics:  a.   With a few exceptions, they are mentally, emotionally, and physically mature.  b.   Most men have a serious purpose and are eager to get the most from their  training if they are motivated properly.  c.   They are keenly interested   in the practical applications of theory and knowledge.  They judge instruction in terms of their needs and the demands of their jobs.  They are interested in the why and how of what they are asked to do.  d.   They are quick to appreciate and respect instructors   who know their subject and  who have the knack of effective presentation. They are equally quick to detect the  incompetent.  e.   Men vary in their physical characteristics, intelligence, general education, past  experience,   determination   or   desire   to   achieve,   and   emotional   stability.  Instructors must be aware of these differences and take them into account.  However, most men are capable of mastering the essentials of military training if  they are well taught.  4.   Advice to Instructors .   There are certain fundamental pr inciples which instructors  must follow if they are to be successful.  a.   Never bluff to cover lack of knowledge. Instructors must know their subjects  thoroughly; but even then, questions may arise to which they do not know the  answers. If they do not know the   answer, admit it; then find the correct answer  and give it to the class as soon as practicable.  b.   Never use profanity or obscenity. If you do, you lose dignity and class respect.  c.   Never use sarcasm or ridicule. Since students are helpless to retort, they beco me  resentful: when an individual is resentful, his mind is closed to learning.  d.   Never talk down to a class. Make your class feel that you consider yourself  fortunate to have acquired the experience and knowledge which you wish to  share with fellow members o f your profession.

--- PAGE 123 ---
123  RESTD  RESTD  e.   Never lose patience. Slowness or inability to grasp a meaning may mean that the  instructor has failed to teach, not that the student has failed to learn.  f.   Remember that learning is acquired to ensure success in battle. Use every  opportuni ty to impress students with the battle importance of what they are  learning.  Section II   -   THE INSTRUCTOR  5.   Qualifications of the Good Instructor .   There are certain qualifications which an  instructor must possess if he is to do an effective job of teaching .  a.   Knowledge of the Subject .   It is obvious that the instructor must know his  subject if he is to teach it to others. As far as possible, instructors should have  both field experience and a thorough knowledge of the applicable training  literature.   An   instruc tor makes   a serious mistake if   he assumes   that field  experience   alone   gives   him   sufficient   knowledge   to   teach   without   further  preparation and continuous study of training literature. Field experience should  enable him to evaluate the material in training m anuals and to present it in a  manner which will be realistic to students. He should know more about his  subject than he will ever have time to teach and, certainly, should be prepared to  answer virtually any question on the subject.  b.   Knowledge of Techniques   of Instruction .   A knowledge of how to instruct is a  definite   prerequisite   to   good   instruction   and   is - the   reason   for   conducting  instructor training courses in the Army.  c.   Personality of the Instructor .   A good personality is considered essential to  success in   all fields of endeavour in which people have to work together and  become closely associated. Personality can be defined as the sum total of all of  those things about an individual to which other people respond, either favourably  or unfavourably. A good pe rsonality - one which gets a favourable response - is not  some mysterious inborn quality. It can be developed by concentrating upon and  improving specific features of the personality. Each instructor should observe  other instructors whenever he can, and weigh   their personality characteristics  against his own. He should then strive to develop in himself those characteristics  which contribute to successful teaching, and to avoid those characteristics which  interfere with effective instruction.  d.   Leadership  (1)   Instruct ors who are good leaders can develop proper habits, attitudes,  appreciations, and character traits in their students as well as teach the  basic information required in the course. They will see that discipline is  maintained and that students conduct themse lves as soldiers at all times.  They will maintain control of their classes and see that the classes run  smoothly.  (2)   An instructor must have leadership ability if he is to manage a class in an  efficient manner. This management includes detailed planning of th e  course,   securing   and   distributing   supplies   and   equipment,   keeping  records, reducing waste, adjusting the program to the needs of students,  making reports, and a number of other administrative details which are

--- PAGE 124 ---
124  RESTD  RESTD  necessary for effective teaching. Classes mu st be managed before they can  be taught.  e.   Professional Attitude  (1)   The Army instructor must have a genuine and sincere interest in students  and their success, in his subject and methods of teaching it, and in the  specific   training   program   to   which   he   is   assign ed.   To   succeed,   the  instructor   must   have   a   sympathetic   understanding   of   his   students'  problems and be fair in dealing with each individual.  (2)   The instructor who has the proper professional attitude will continually  add to his store of knowledge and skills in   his subject and make every  effort to improve his teaching ability.  (3)   Everything an instructor says and does during classes, as well as the  manner in which he says and does things, reflects his attitude toward his  students, his subject, and the training pro - gram. His attitudes have a  tremendous   influence   upon   student   attitudes   and   morale,   because  students tend to adopt both the attitude of the instructor and his point of  view toward the subject and the training.  6.   How the Instructor Can Improve .   Any instructor   can improve himself by bringing  about desirable changes in specific aspects of his qualifications; but he can do this only by  constant effort. The fact that an instructor has taught for some time does not mean that he has  made significant improvement, nor   does the fact that an instructor is adjudged superior this  year mean that he will be superior next year.  a.   Know What Makes Good Instruction .   In   his   efforts   to   improve,   the  instructor   must start   with   an   appreciation   of   the   basic   elements   of   good  instruction.   He must set certain standards for himself.  b.   Observe Other Instructors .   The instructor must maintain his own individuality  while improving his technique. He should observe other instructors for the  purpose of learning from them, but probably should not imita te even the most  capable ones to too great an extent. Two highly competent instructors can have  radically different personalities and use entirely different techniques, and both  still be able to do a superior job of teaching. Instructors should determine w hat  they can do well and then develop techniques based on their own outstanding  abilities rather than on abilities which they admire but lack.  c.   Analyse Your Own Characteristics .   Self - evaluation and self - analysis are  essential to improvement. The instructor   should constantly attempt to evaluate  his own teaching and analyze his own characteristics to find out just what his  strengths and weaknesses are. Discover strengths and build upon them; discover  weaknesses and correct them.  d.   Concentrate on Specific Element s .   Progress   is   made   by   concentrating   upon  specific techniques and working out a systematic plan for their improvement. A  general attitude on the part of an instructor to "do good," while commendable,  rarely brings the significant improvement desired; atten tion must be given to  improving specific aspects of instruction.  e.   Seek Help of Associates .   An   instructor   cannot always   evaluate his  own work objectively. He should encourage others to criticize his instruction and  should welcome their suggestions. Other in structors, assistant instructors, and

--- PAGE 125 ---
125  RESTD  RESTD  supervisors can identify an instructor's   strengths and weaknesses more readily  then can the instructor himself.  f.   Make Constant Effort To Improve .   The instructor's attitude toward his work is  best judged by the effort he   makes to improve. After each lesson, the good  instructor will ask himself how he could have done the job better. He will not be  satisfied with anything but the best. Constant alertness in seeking the best  methods to improve his men's learning is the most   important single ingredient in  the instructor's recipe for improvement.  Section III   -   STAGES OF INSTRUCTION  7.   General .   The process involved in teaching, which includes the five stages of  instruction, is the foundation on which a single lesson or an entire s ubject is built.  a.   Preparation by the Instructor .  (1)   Careful planning is the first step in efficient training. The instructor must  analyze the specific procedures, skills, and information which are to be  taught. He must organize materials so that the basic ide as are adapted to  the student's backgrounds and needs and so that the lessons are aimed at  achieving specific objectives. He must present the lesson in the manner  that will best facilitate learning; the objective of the lesson will determine  the methods to   be used. He must proceed from the simple to the complex,  from the known to the unknown.  (2)   Careful preliminary analysis, correct solution of all instructional problems,  and repeated rehearsal and review of procedures and materials -   will help  to insure maximu m student   - learning in the minimum amount of time.  (3)   Mastery of the subject is only the first step in instructor preparation. The  instructor must determine how to arouse the students' desire to learn and  how to present subject material so that each member of   the class learns all  essential procedures and ideas.  b.   Presentation.   Actual teaching begins with the second stage of instruction, which  is the presentation stage.  (1)   Introduction .   Students must irst be prepared to receive the ideas which  will be made available   to them; their attention must be attracted, their  enthusiasm aroused. They must be told what they are to learn, why they  are learning this particular material, and the proficiency they are expected  to   attain.   Use   of   brief   and   familiar   illustrations,   exper iences,   and  comparisons will aid in accomplishing the desired results.  (2)   Explanation . Following the introduction, the explanation makes new  ideas available to students. This is accomplished by lectures, conferences,  or discussions, all of which should lead t o comparisons or contrasts in  terms   of   what students already know, and by graphic, step - by - step  illustrations. The instructor must remember that telling, through lectures  and conferences, is limited to the sense of hearing. A combination of  techniques util izing several senses sight, smell, touch, hearing - results in  faster learning and must be employed wherever possible.  (3)   Demonstration .   Another   method   of   presenting   new   material   is   by  showing. This is of particular importance in Army instruction because

--- PAGE 126 ---
126  RESTD  RESTD  soldie rs must be shown how to do the jobs they will perform. Good  demonstrations leave a vivid impression on the student.  c.   Application .   In this stage students are given an opportunity to do, to apply the  principles and procedures learned in the presentation. Ef ficient use of application  periods will often give students a firm grasp of a difficult subject. Application  periods must always be supervised to ensure that students follow correct  procedures. Team performance as well as individual performance can be grea tly  improved in this stage.  d.   Examination .   In   this   stage,   instructors   check   on   the   students'   mastery   of  materials taught. Instructors can be certain that students have mastered the  essentials only by checking their ability   to perform without assistance.  e.   Revi ew and/or Critique .   This is the final stage of instruction and always  follows   the   application   or   the   examination.   Here   instructors   review   the  knowledge, skills, techniques, or appreciations acquired in the instructional unit.  This stage completes the pictu re by clarifying any phases of instruction which are  not completely understood. The term critique is usually restricted to the review  given after an examination or applicatory exercise.  8.   Application of the Stages of Instruction .   The stages of instruction se rve as a check list  for the instructor in choosing teaching procedures. Whenever practical, he applies all five stages  to each lesson presented. It is often better to present certain subjects in small segments,  processing each segment through all the stage s of instruction, including the application and  examination stages, before going to the next segment. In some lessons various stages are  combined; for example, in many practical exercises the application and examination stages are  combined. In controlled p ractice, when material is presented step by step, the presentation and  application stages are combined. Flexibility is the key to successful use of the stages of  instruction. The instructor should use them as a guide; they are not intended to cause  instruc tion to become artificial or stereotyped. However, the instructor must study every  instructional situation for opportunities to secure student participation in the application,  examination, and critique stages. He must strive for the complete teaching proc ess in which he  can plan, tell, show, do, check, and review and/or critique.  BASIC REQs OF INSTRS  GSP - 1929  1.   Gen .   The mil instr has a definite aim. It is to make students learn something new, to  improve their previous knowledge or to impart adv trg. For eff icient instr, a successful contract  has to be made b/w the instr and the student. After the period is over the instr should have a  sense of achievements and the students must have benefit. If this is not achieved the whole  period of instr will be wastage.   Basic reqs are listed below: -  2.   Types of Instrs .   The mil instr can be divided into the fol types: -  a. The classroom is a dynamic envmt, bringing together students   from  diff   bkgr   with   various   abilities   and   personalities. Vis.  b.   Coop learning.  c.   Inquiry   based instr.  d.   Differentiation.  e.   Tech adv.  f.   Behavior mgmt.  g.   Professional dev.  7.   Teaching Methodology .   Teaching and learning are the two sides of a coin. The  most accepted criterion for measuring good teaching is the amount of student learning that  occurs. Th ere are consistently high correlation b/w students’ ratings of the “amount learned” in  the course and their overall ratings of the teacher and the course. There are diff types of  teaching methods that can be categorized into four broad types: -  a.   Teacher cen   methods  b.   Learner cen methods  c.   Content focused methods

--- PAGE 128 ---
128  RESTD  RESTD  d.   interactive / participative methods  Note: -   Difference b/w Strategy And Method .   Strategy   is how you plan to do something, and   methods   are how   you  actually do it . Strategy means how to select things and go als, while the method is meaning how to implicate these things  and achieve them. A plan of action designed to achieve an overall aim. Method is a way something is done  PREP OF PRESN/ LECTURES  1.   Def.   The lecture method of presentation in one in which the inst r dev the subject  entirely by himself without much of cl participation.  2. Prep th e final notes (Lesson Plan) by arranging  ideas into logical order under main and sub hdgs.  c.   It is advisable not to use the complete text of the lecture during the class. It  should be reduced to brief notes, taking only the outline.  d.   A rehearsal in some form   is advisable, especially if it is your first lecture. This will  also help you finish your lecture in time.  e.   Prep the trg aids according to the subj.  f.   It is good to issue a student précis for a lecture. Decide the time of issue.  7.   Presentation Tech  a.   It is vital   to interest the audience from the very beginning, Plan the apch, it must  be lively, interesting and thought provoking.  b.   Make best use of your voice and manners.  c.   To overcome “Saturation” promote class participation and bring humor, ghost  voice, tape recorder , epidiascope, viewgraph, maps and such other aids will help.  d.   Be enthusiastic, Make your audience feel that you know your stuff and that you  are enjoying talking to them.  e.   Be absolutely natural.  f.   Summarize when there is need to clarify or review. As a gen ru le summarize after  every three teaching pts. End up a brief resume of complete presentation.

129  RESTD  RESTD  8.   Modern Tech Of   Prep a Lec .   Successful lecturers are not simply those with the most  expertise or the most outgoing personalities. Knowledge of the subj and co mfort in public  speaking are helpful, but a lec is only successful if it comm the material eff to the listeners.  Thoughtful planning is the key. This process can be broken into four parts: -  a.   Defining And Limiting The Topic .   The topic of the lecture may be   assigned or  left to your discretion. Even when the topic is given in the syllabus, there still is  latitude in terms of what angle you take and which aspects you choose to  highlight. In other words, what should students know or be able to do at the end  of y our lecture?  b.   Structure and Clarity .   A   brilliant material wrapped in a convoluted package is  not a successful lecture. Consider issues of clarity from your perspective first. You  must clearly org your thoughts in your own mind. If the structure is not clear   to  you, then it certainly will not be clear to your students.   Think also about clarity  from the student’s perspective. It is str advisable to give the students a roadmap  for your lecture.  c.   Elms of Preplanning .   Whether you lecture from an outline or from a   script, it is  important to verify that you have the right amount of info. Once you begin to time  the lecture, you may find that you have to go back and narrow your topic.  Consider the pace at which you are speaking, and be sure to vary your del in  order to   avoid monotony for the listener.  d.   Use of Teaching Aids .   When used well, handouts, examples, ppt, images and  graphs can illustrate complex ideas succinctly. They can prompt discussion, and  the change of pace can keep students more interested. On the down s ide, these  same tools can also be a distraction. Handouts and ppt presentations that are not  accurately sync to the lecture can cause confusion. Use these items judiciously  and intentionally.  USE OF TRG AIDS  1.   Intro .   Learning is a systemic and cont proce ss. Diff methods and tech are being used to  prom better understanding. Trg aids are the basic component of learning in army. Trg aids helps  students to understand complex problems in a better way. 133  RESTD  RESTD  d.   Keep Aids Covered When They are not in Use .   Large charts can be covered by  the simple expedient of stapling sheets of wrapping paper over them. If the chart  contains lines of printing, strips of paper can be cut to the size of   each line. These  strips can be removed one at a time. Machinery, wpns and similar aids can be  covered with tgt cloth or similar material if need be. This will avoid distracting  student’s attn during instr.  e. If the aid is a chart, view it from the rear of  the cl room to make sure it is legible to everyone in the cl. In many instances, a  change in the seating arrangement will enable the stu dents to see better. The  finest aid is of no value if the students cannot see it.  f.   Do not Obstruct the Students View of the Aid .   Do not stand in front of the aid;  do not put it at an angle.  g.   Talk to the Cl Not to the Aid .   Some instrs becauseome so invo lved with  their trg aids that they completely forget the students. Even while disassembling  a piece of eqpt, the instr should maint eye contact with his cl. When explaining a  chart or blackbd drawing, stand as nearly as possible on the side of the aid, thi s  will help eliminate any tendency of talking to the aid rather than to the cl. When  drawing on the blackbd, finish the drawing quickly then face the cl and explain  the pt.  h.   Use of Pointer .   In most instances, a pointer is highly desirable in focusing  the   student’s attn on a particular part of the trg aid. When using the pter, hold it  steadily on the part of the aid which you want the cl to observe. Holding the pter  in the hand nearest to the aid will enable you to maint better eye contact with the  cl, hold ing the pter across the body tends to cause you to talk to the aid rather  than to the students.  i.   Use Assts to Your Best Adv .   If you use asstinstrs, make sure they are well  rehearsed so that they will know exactly what they are to do and when. If an asst  i s to show projected aids for you, have a prearngsig so that he will know when to  change slides or cut off the machine.  j.   Display Aids Smoothly .   When using three or four large charts or sliding bds, no  the bds in the order they are to be used, if hyeqpt is t o be brought in or out during  the cl make sure it can be moved in quietly without undue disturbance.  k.   The Use of Blackbd .   Some hints on how to use the blackbd are: -  (1)   Check on the eqpt to be used.  (2)   Check for glare.  (3)   Keep blackbd clean.  (4)   Plan your work in a dv.  (5)   Keep material simple and brief.  (6)   Print and draw legibly.  (7)   Use colour for emphasis and variety.  (8)   Prep complicated illustrations beforehand and cover them with paper.  (9)   Do not crowd your work, if necessary.

134  RESTD  RESTD  PREP OF SYL AND TRG PROGM  1.   Intro .   Syl   and trg p rogm serve as aid both to the Instr and his class. They are an integral  part of all trg. A great deal of careful planning and forethought is required for the prep of syl and  trg progm. The success of trg and a course of instruction is largely dependent on   how well and  carefully he syl was chalked out.  2.   Gen Consd & A dv of   P rep a   S yl and   T rg   P rogm .   It   incl   the   aim   of   the   course,  duration, parade timings pds aval for trg, details of students, instructions, demo tps, adm etc.  The advantages of prep a syl and t rg progm for conducting any trg are -  a.   Progressiveness.  b.   Continuity.  c.   Completeness.  d.   Forecast.  3.   Stages of Prep   of Trg Progm .   The seq of action by a person detailed to prep the  syl and trg progm is: -  a.   Planning .   Study the directive carefully and be clear as   to the aim and scope of  the course of instruction. The directive of commanding officer's policy must  include the fol: -  (1)   Aim of the course.  (2)   Duration.  (3)   Number of students.  b.   Having clarified the aim and scope of the course, you should consider the fol  facto rs: -  (1)   Facilities aval for trg.  (2)   Hours of work.  (3)   Likely interruptions or cmts.  (4)   Time of the yr, weather etc.  (5)   Pre - course stds of students.  c.   Paper work  (1)   Gen consideration.  (2)   Detailed syl.  (3)   Block time table.  (4)   Weekly trg progm.  4.   T ypes of   S y l .   There are two ki nds of syllabi, block and detailed one.  a.   The block syl contains list of subjects necessary to achieve the aim incl the  revision, testing, adm, spare pds and code name of each subj.  b.   In detailed syl, the actual pds req to teach lessons, within the subj, i n accordance  with the aim and scope of the subj are worked out.  5.   P lan of   A ction while   P rep   Syl .   The   block   and   detailed   syl   should   be   prep  concurrently. The plan of action at this stage should be as under -  a.   List of the subjs in their importance, necessary t o achieve the aim, incl misc and  spare pds.  b.   Make out the detailed syl in rough for each subj (keep the aim in view).  c.   At first base your planning on the time required to teach subj and not the time  that is aval.  d.   Add up the total number of pds req and com pare it with actual number of pds  aval.

--- PAGE 135 ---
135  RESTD  RESTD  e.   Normally you will need more pds than allotted.  f.   Review each subject once again, remove relatively un - important pds but not  essential pds.  g.   If you are, still short of time, consider if you can cut more of the subjs w ithout  affecting the aim of the course.  h.   If this is not possible, then you or your comd (if he has decided on the aim) must  give more time, accept a lower stds (usually not desirable) or revise the aim. A  limited amount taught properly is generally far bet ter than a greater amount  poorly taught (see Annexes E and G for layout   -   Page 160 & 164 of GSP Techs of  Mil Instrs).  6.   Block Time Table .   The prep of "Block Time Table" is the intermediate step ldg to the  prep of the weekly trg progm. It ensures that the su bjs and the lessons within each subj are in  logical and progressive teaching order. It serves as a long rg forecast and acts as an arithmetical  check upon periods. The breakdown of pds should be done on 'weekly block'. If the course is of  few days duration   then it can be broken down straight away into 'Daily Block'.  a.   Consds : -  (1)   If the subjects are easy, the bulk of pure instructions should come early,  leaving plenty of subsequent pds for revision and testing.  (2)   In case of difficult subjs, the instruction sho uld be spread more evenly  over whole syl with regular gaps for revision and testing.  (3)   Both the instr and the students will make slow progress at first. Do not put  too many subjs at a time.  (4)   In order that unforeseen interruptions and slow progress in certa in subjs  can be catered for, arrange most of the spare periods towards the end of  the syl.  (5)   Do not put two or more similar subjects side by side; it will give learner no  incentive for learning.  (6)   Build up the syl to and with a good climax which will give le arner an  incentive for learning.  (7)   In arranging syl for a long course, the working tempo should reach a  number of climaxes each followed by a period of relaxation (Annex H for  layout   -   Page 165 of GSP Techs of Mil Instrs).  7.   Weekly Trg Programme .   Programmes   are usually best made out on a weekly basis.  In addition, a daily programme, if well set out, can do much to arouse interest. Unnecessary  details are not required to be included in the programme.  a.   Consds : -  (1)   Progm are an aid to instruction, therefore, they   must be simple to  understand, accurate in details and attractive to see.  (2)   Must be issued well in adv to allow time for both instr and students to  prep.  (3)   Pds may have to be 'staggered' to off set lack of eqpt, shortage of instrs, ltd  areas and class accn e tc.  (4)   Variety in presentation is essential.  (5)   Max active pds should altn with non - active pds and boring work with  interesting work. Each day should be planned to end with a good climax.

--- PAGE 136 ---
136  RESTD  RESTD  (6)   Put hardest physical and hardest mental work in the beginning and most  interesting work at the end of the day. Appl of this principle will depend  upon the nature of work and climatic condns.  (7)   Breaks are an efficient method of eliminating phy and mental fatigue. One  long break is better than many short ones.  (8)   Allow sufficient   time for move between the pds from class to class.  (9)   Avoid lectures immediately after lunch.

When   the   pin   is  pulled out and the gren is thrown , the lever files off,  releasing the striker, which acting under the influence of  its spring, performs like a spring wire of mousetrap,  swinging and striking the cap of the igniter set. A flash is  caused and this ignites t he fuze, which burns from b/w  2.5 - 4 sec. This in turn sets off the detonator, which  bursts the body of the gren and thus exposes the inside  fillings to the air.  8.   Igniter Set .   Igniter set consists of a 0.22 - inch  cap, cap chamber, a short length of safety f uze and a  detonator.

Before firing th e shroud, which is loc in the cylindrical housing  of the metal cone cover the main detonator blocks the passage in b/w the two  detonators and acts as a safety device during storage and tn. On firing due to set  back force, the shroud over comes the shroud s pring and mov to the rear. On  deceleration, the safety cap of shroud under pressure from spring, is thrown  towards the front and thus clear the passage leading to the main detonator.  c.   Percussion Action of Fuze .   On impact, the striker over comes the s triker  spring and fires the fuze detonator from where the detonations/flash wave is  passed on to the bottom main detonator which in turn detonates the relay  booster as well as the main firing.  d.   Graze Action .   If the gren does not hit the tgt, when due to gr aze action, the  detonator contained in the detonator assy creeps fwd and hits the striker thus  resulting in its detonation which in turn causes the detonation of the bottom  detonator and main filling.  12.   Safety Devices  a.   Fol safety devices have been incorpora ted in the gren (To be completed in single  line):   -

PRINCIPLES OF THROWING GREN  Handling  1.   It is further divided into fol parts:   -  a.   The Ready Posn .   The gren is picked up  and held in rt hand with an over hand grip.  Pt out that the lever is uppermost with the  fingers over it and the Ist or 2n d finger of  the lt hand is placed through the ring of the  safety pin. The gren is held to the body  above the waist. The body is then turned to  the rt so that the lt shoulder and foot are  towards the tgt. Pt out if anyone is a lt  handed thrower then the gre n will be held  in the lt hand.  b.   Throwing Action .   To throw the gren the  lt arm is kept still & close to the body & by  vigorously thrusting the rt arm backwards  and downwards, the pin is pulled out. The  thrower must look down at the gren to  make sure tha t the safety pin has come out. Looking in the dir of the tgt and  keeping the lt shoulder pting in that dir, the body swung backwards as far as  possible allowing the lt hand and lt foot if nec to come up naturally. Then without  any pause, the body and strai ght rt arm are swung quickly fwd, the gren being rel  as the hand reaches its highest pt above the shoulder. The fall of the gren must be  watched.

The fall of gren must be watched.  d.   The lt handed throwers should be made to stand on the rt side of the line.  3.   Corrn of Faults .   Fol mistakes are commonly observed while throwing, which  must be watched and corrected:   -  a.   Lo ss of dist because the gren is being rel either before the hand reaches, or after it  has passed the highest pt above the shoulders.  b.   The gren falling wide because the shoulders are not kept in line with the tgt.

Operating Instuctions .   Prior to shooting, a check is to be made to ensure t hat the  telescope is mounted on the weapon correctly. Afterwards, steps given below are followed: -  a.   Focusing .   Focusing of the eyepiece is accomplished by turning the eye piece.  To focus optimally, proceed as follows: -  (1)   Turn the eyepiece anti - clockwise until   it stops.  (2)   Look through the scope   -   the correct distance between eyepiece and eye is  about 80 mm.  (3)   Turn the eyepiece clockwise until the image of the reticle pattern is  sharply focused.  (4)   The dioptric correction rg is +/   -   25 diopter.  b.   Elevation adjustmen t .   Elevation   adjustment   is   carried   out   with   the  elevation turret on the top of the scope. One click corresponds to a shift of the line  of sight of 0.25 mil. The elevation drum is scaled and numbered in clicks from 0 to  40. A conversion table shows the corr esponding number of clicks to the target  distances.  c.   Windage adjustment .   The   turret  for windage adjustment is placed on the  right side of the scope and allows fine  lateral correction of the point of impact  in order to correct for side wind. The  middle adj ustment point is when the  figure “0” is opposite to the reference  index.   Clockwise   adjustment   of   the  windage turret (in the direction of “R”)  move the point of impact to the rightt.  Anti - clock   wise   adjustment   (in   the  direction   of   “L”) moves   the   point   of  im pact   to   the   left.   One   click  corresponds to 0.1 mils leftt or right .  The adjustment range is +/   -   2 mils.  d.   Aiming Procedure .   Aim to the target with the center of the reticle pattern. The  width of the inner horizontal CL corresponds to 0.15 mils. The width   of the  vertical line and the outer horizontal lines corresponds to 1.5 mils. The clear  space between the arrow and the central horizontal line corresponds to 0.7 mils.  The interval between the thick horizontal lines correspond to 14 mils.  1.   Elevation Knob  a.   Coin   Screw  b.   Elevation Turret.  c.   Elevation   Scale  Drum  d.   Reference   Index  2.   Windage Knob  e.   Reference Index  f.   Windage   Scale  Drum  g.   Windage Turret  h.   Coin Screw
