158  RESTD  RESTD  FIELD SKILLS  (Note: This lesson is applicable for all sniper wpns, i.e Steyr Sniper Rfl as well)  Camouflage  1.   Camouflage is one of the basic weapons of war. To the sniper team, it can   mean the  difference between life and death. Camouflage   measures are important since the team cannot  afford to be detected at any time while moving alone, as part of another element or while op  from a firing position whereas marksmanship training teaches th e sniper to hit a target.  Knowing how and when to Camouflage can enable the sniper to avoid becoming a target. He  must be camouflage conscious from the time he departs on a mission until he returns. Paying  attention to Camouflage fundamentals is a mark of   a well - trained sniper.  2.   Fundamentals of Camouflage .   The sniper must pay careful attention when using  Camouflage clothing and equipment (artificial and   natural). He should apply the following  fundamental rules when determining his Camouflage needs:   -  a.   Take ad vantage of all available natural concealment such as trees, bushes, grass,  earth, man - made structures, and shadows.  b.   Alter the form, shadow, texture and colour of objects.  c.   Camouflage against ground and air observation.  d.   Camouflage a sniper post by keeping fo llowing points in mind:   -  (1)   Study the terrain and vegetation in the area. Arrange grass, leaves, brush  and other natural Camouflage to conform to the area.  (2)   Use only as much material as is needed. Excessive use of material (natural  or artificial) can reveal a   sniper's position.  (3)   Obtain natural material over a wide area. Do not strip an area as this may  attract the enemy's attention.  e.   Dispose of excess soil by covering it with leaves and grass or by dumping it under  bushes, into streams or into ravines. Piles of   fresh dirt indicate that an area is  occupied and reduce the effectivness of camouflage.  f.   The   sniper   and   his   equipment   must   blend   with   the   natural   background.  Remember that vegetation changes colour many times in an area.  Camouflage in Different Geographical   Areas  3.   Deployment with Reqular Troops .   When deployed with regular troops in an urban  area, the sniper should be dressed like the   troops in the area. When the sniper is in position, he  should be camouflaged to match his area of operations. He can use a bulk y, shapeless, gray  Camouflage suit that has been coloured to match rubble and debris. He should make sure some  type of hood breaks up the outline of the head during daylight hours & he should be extremely  slow and careful, if at all, because of the unlimit ed amount of possible enemy sniper positions.  4.   Desert Areas .   In sandy and desert areas that have minimal vegetation, textured  camouflage is normally not necessary. Still,   proper colouring of a suit that breaks up the sniper's  human outline is needed. Blend ing tan and brown colours is most effective. A bulky - type smock  of light material with a hood works well.  5.   Jungle Areas .   In jungle areas, the sniper can use foliage, artificial Camouflage and  Camouflage paint in a contrasting pattern that   will blend with   the texture of the terrain. In a  very hot and humid area, he should wear only a light camouflage suit. A heavy suit will cause a  loss of too much body fluid. The vegetation is usually very thick in jungle areas so the sniper can  rely more on the natural fo liage for concealment.

--- PAGE 159 ---
159  RESTD  RESTD  Camouflage Discipline  6.   Basic Methods of Cam .   The   sniper   must   constantly   observe   the   terrain   and  vegetation   changes   to pick   the most concealed   routes of advance and be certain   he is  camouflaged properly. He should use shadows caused   by vegetation, terrain features and man  made features   to   remain   undetected.   The sniper   team   can   use three basic methods   of  Camouflage. It may use one of these methods or a comb of all three to accomplish its objective.  The three basic methods a sniper tea m can use are  hiding, blending and deceiving.  a.   Hiding .   This technique enables the  sniper   to   completely   conceal   his   body  from observation. Hiding may be used if  the sniper stumbles upon an enemy patrol  and immediate concealment is needed or  if he wishes to   stay out of sight during daylight hours to await darkness.  b.   Blending .   A   sniper   should   use   this  tech since it is not possible to completely  camouflage in such a way as to be identical  with   the   surrounding   area.   Camouflage  needs   to be so nearly   perfect that   the  sniper   cannot   be   recognized   through  optical gear or even with the human eye.  He must be able to be looked at directly  and not be seen. This trait takes much practice and experience. The   ghillie suit   is  a form of blending.  c.   Deceiving .   In this method, th e sniper tricks the enemy into a false conclusion  regarding his location, intentions or movement. By planting objects such as  empty cases, food packing material or something attracting attention, the sniper  decoys the enemy into the open where he can be br ought under fire. Cutting  enemy communications wire and waiting for the repair personnel is an example.  Ghillie Suit  7.   The ghillie suit is a specially made Camouflage uniform that is covered with irregular  patterns of garnish or netting.   Ghillie suits can b e made from uniforms. Turning the uniform  inside out places the pockets inside the suit. This protects items in the pockets from damage  caused by crawling on the ground. The front of the ghillie suit should be covered with some type  of heavy cloth to reinf orce it. The knees and elbows should be covered with two layers of canvas  and the seam of the crotch should be reinforced with heavy nylon thread since these areas are  prone to wear out  quicker.  8.   A veil (over  suit covering back  only) can be made  from a net   or piece  of   cloth   covered  with   garnish   or  netting.   It   covers  the   weapon   and

--- PAGE 160 ---
160  RESTD  RESTD  sniper's head when in a firing position. The sniper can sew the veil into the ghillie suit or he can  carry it separately. The sniper must remember that a ghillie suit does not make   him invisible but  is only a camouflage base. The sniper can add natural vegetation to help blend with the  surroundings at a rate of   60 to 70 percent natural to 30 to 40 percent man - made .  9.   Ghillie Suit Limitations  a.   Ghillie Suits are heavy and cause the body   to heat up quickly in hot climate,  therefore putting the sniper team at risk of dehydration. Because of this, one  should always wear it during a stalk and drink sufficient water to prevent  dehydration.  b.   Ghillie suits are vulnerable to fire and hence req spe cial care and attention.  c.   Being heavy it is difficult and cumbersome to move in a ghillie suit.  COVER AND CONCEALMENT  Cover and Concealment  1.   Cover .   A cover is natural or artificial   protection from the fire   of enemy weapons.  Natural cover (ravines, hollows , reverse slopes   etc) and artificial cover (fighting posns, trenches,  and walls etc) protect the sniper team from flat trajectory fires and partly protect it from high -  angle fires. Even the smallest depression or fold in the ground may provide some cover w hen  the team needs it most. A   6 - inch   depression, properly used, may provide enough cover to save  the sniper team under fire. Snipers must always look for and take advantage of all the cover that  the terrain provides. By combining this habit with proper mov ement techniques, the team can  protect itself from enemy fire.  2.   Concealment .   Natural   or   artificial   protection   from   enemy   observation   is  concealment. The surroundings may provide natural   concealment that needs no change before  use (bushes, grass and shadow s etcetera). The sniper team creates artificial concealment from  materials such as jute strips (garnish) and camouflage nets or it can move natural materials  (bushes, leaves and grass etc) from their original location. The sniper team must consider the  eff ects of the change of season on the concelment provided by both natural and artificial  materials. The principles of concealment include the following: -  a.   Avoid unnecessary movement.  b.   Use all available concealment.  c.   Stay low to observe.  d.   Expose nothing that shi nes.  e.   Avoid sky lining.  f.   Alter familiar outlines.  g.   Keep quiet; noise discipline.  Sniper Team Movement  3.   Rules of Movement . When moving, the sniper should always remember the following  rules: -  a.   Always assume that the area is under enemy observation.  b.   Move slowly , progress by feet and inches.  c.   Do not cause the overhead movement of trees, bushes or tall grasses by rubbing  against them.  d.   Plan every move and traverse the route in segments.  e.   Stop, look and listen often.

--- PAGE 161 ---
161  RESTD  RESTD  Considerations for Moving and Occupation of Firing   Position  4.   The sniper team cannot afford to be seen at any time by anyone. Therefore, its movement  will be slow and deliberate. The movement   over any given distance will be considerably slower.  Stealth is a sniper's security. The sniper team must consider fo llowing points for movement: -  a.   Due to the increased weight of the system, ammunition and team equipment,  vehicle move is desirable as far as possible.  b.   Covered route to the position be ensured. Amount of crawling is reduced due to  heavy weight and conspicuou s shape.  c.   Better selection of withdrawal routes (after the shot, the sniper becomes a higher  priority target and must select route for quick egress)  d.   Move during hours of darkness on a carefully planned and chosen route.  e.   Move during disturbances such as gunf ire, explosions, aircraft noise, wind or  anything that will distract the enemy's attention or conceal the team's movement.  5.   Consider following while occupying a firing position: -  a.   Much larger signature of the rifle, clear area and dampen soil.  b.   Concealed posi tion for effective re - engagement of the target.  c.   Ensure local protection of the position.  d.   Steady position and a clear line of sight.  e.   Stand - off from enemy light sniper rifle range i.e. at least 600 - 800 metres.  EMPLOYMENT OF SNIPERS IN CONVENTIONAL   & SUB - C ONVENTIONAL OPERATIONS  ( GSP   -   1325 )  1.   Intro .   Snipers are precision target engagement weapons. These weapons have very high  degree of accuracy. They also provide capability to engage key personnel at a stand - off distance,  especially enemy snipers.  2.   The   prima ry mission   of snipers is to   support combat operations by delivering precise  fire   on selected targets from forward concealed positions.   Secondary mission   is   collecting and  reporting   Battlefield   information.   They   avoid   sustained   battles.   Sniper   weapon   inflic ts  casualties, slows enemy movement, causes harassment, lowers morale and adds uncertainty /  confusion in the enemy lines. This role becomes more significant when the target is entrenched  or positioned among civilians, or during Sub Conventional Operations . Fire of automatic  weapons in such operations can result in wounding or killing of non - combatants. Thus, there is a  requirement to precisely see the employment of sniper in different forms of combat.  3.   Section/ Platoon Designated Marksman.   Section/ Platoon   designated marksmen are  not professional snipers. They are fully integrated members of the Rifle Section/ Platoon who  provide an improved capability for the rifle company. They do not operate semi - independently  on the Battlefield as snipers, nor do they r outinely engage targets at the extreme range common  to snipers. The designated marksman employ general - purpose weapon. He also receives  training available within the unit’s resources to improve the section/ platoon’s precision  engagement capacities at shor t and medium ranges.  4.   A   rifleman   may be assigned   as   the designated   marksman.   He is   chosen for   his  demonstrated shooting ability, maturity, reliability, good judgment, and experience. Decision  making must be able to execute the entire range of individual an d collective rifleman tasks  within the section/ platoon.

--- PAGE 162 ---
162  RESTD  RESTD  5.   Conceptual Contours of Employment  a.   Definition .   A sniper is a special soldier who has been trained to the highest  levels of fieldcraft. He has the ability to search and engage the enemy, identif y key  personnel and equipment, gather and report real time information and can apply  accurate shot placement at the enemy.  b.   Employment .   Snipers are employed in all types and levels of conflict. This  includes Defense and Attk in which precision fire is d elivered at longer ranges,  withdrawal operations in which snipers are part of forces Ieft in contact or as  stay behind forces. It also includes minor operations such as patrols, ambushes,  and   counter   sniper   operations.   They   also   have   an   important   role   in   S ub  Conventional Operations, Built Up Area Clearance and Anti Terrorist Operations.  c.   Understanding Commander’s Intent .   The sniper team must understand  commander’s intent, his concept of operation and purpose of the assigned task.  This understanding allo ws the team to exercise initiative within the framework of  commander’s   intent   and   to   support   his   concept   and   subsequently  accomplishment of unit’s mission.  d.   Freedom of Action .   Snipers must have freedom of action to choose their own  positions once on gr ound. Commander should assign priority to targets so  snipers can avoid involvement in sustained engagements. Regardless of target  priority, sniper team must be free to change targets to support commander’s  intent.  e.   Control & Coordination .   Although Sniper h as to perform different tasks in an  operation, for that he needs to understand the principles and characteristics of  war. Snipers are required to be controlled and coordinated because:   -  (1)   Should know the overall situation and should be briefed carefully.  (2)   Their activities are required to be coordinated with own patrolling and  fire support.  (3)   Snipers should not be given a common task.  (4)   Team’s Weapon .   Snipers should not be employed alone, rather they  should be employed in as a team in pair. They should be in   mutual  cooperation.  f.   Sniper’s Skills  (1)   Marksmanship.  (2)   Range estimation.  (3)   Map Reading.  (4)   Observation.  (5)   Sniper knowledge.  (6)   Special recognition.  (7)   Camouflage and concealment.  (8)   Fieldcraft.  (9)   Intelligence gath ering.  g.   Sniper Selection Criteria  (1)   Marksmanship.  (2)   Vision   and physical condition.  (3)   Smoking discipline.  (4)   Mental condition.

--- PAGE 163 ---
163  RESTD  RESTD  (5)   Intelligence Quotient (IQ).  (6)   Emotional balance.  (7)   Field Craft and tactical Sense.  (8)   Patience and perseverance.  (9)   Knowledge of Math and Science.  (10)   Ability to concentrate/ focus.  h.   Sniper/ Spotter Duties  (1)   Sni per Duties  (a)   Build a steady and comfortable firing position ensuring the natural  point of aim.  (b)   Locate the designated target in the rifle scope.  (c)   Confirm range to the target.  (d)   Repeat firing solution while applying it on the optic to obviate  chances of an inadve rtent mistake.  (e)   Index appropriate elevation and windage on the optic.  (f)   Notify spotter when ready to take the shot.  (g)   Employ fundamentals of marksmanship.  (h)   Make the shot and follow through.  (i)   Call the shot.  (j)   Standby to reengage if necessary.  (2)   Spotter Duties  (a)   Estabish   proper spotting position.  (b)   Identify and designate target / targets.  (c)   Determine range to the target.  (d)   Calculate the precise firing solution.  (e)   Communicate Final Firing Posn to the shooter.  (f)   Order the shot.  (g)   Observe shot through the spotting scope.  (h)   Critique the sh ot / calculate correction.  (i)   Keep track of atmospheric variables as well as wind.  (j)   Order re - engagement if necessary.  Employment in Operations  6.   Defence .   Sniper can be employed in defence in following manner: -  a.   Protective Dettachment s  (1)   Keep constant watch on ene my movement and actions using long range  observation devices.  (2)   Harass   the   enemy   by   delivering   stand - off   precision   fire   on   enemy  elements.  (3)   Engage enemy crew served weapons at long ranges.  (4)   Force the enemy to deploy by targetting command echelons.  (5)   Prevent   enemy closing to and gaining information about own defenses,  engaging his recconnaisance / mobile patrols.  (6)   Assist during disengagement/ withdrawal phase.  b.   Main Defensive Battle  (1)   Prevent close observation of main defenses by the enemy.  (2)   Counter sniping.

--- PAGE 164 ---
164  RESTD  RESTD  (3)   G uard likely enemy approaches.  (4)   Guard likely crossing sites.  (5)   Deny enemy recconnaisance of obstacle system.  (6)   Identify and engage enemy commanders and other key personnel.  7.   Attack  a.   Preperatory Phase  (1)   Obtain information about enemy strength, obstacles and locat ion of major  weapons.  (2)   Loc enemy surveillance system to include standing patrpls, Listening  Posts, Observation Posts and surveillance equipment.  b.   Initial Stage  (1)   Protect own troops in deployed areas against enemy snipers, raids and  spoiling attacks.  (2)   Engage e nemy standing patrols, listening posts.  c.   Assault Stage  (1)   Identify and engage enemy Command Post/ critical signals equipment.  (2)   Engage   Observation   Posts   and   important   enemy   bunkers   through  loopholes.  (3)   Can be employed with Base of Fire elements, particularly Heav y Sniper  Rifle Teams due to their extended range.  (4)   Covering the flanks of the assaulting force  d.   Consolidation/ Exploitation Stage  (1)   Deliver precise fire on targets interfering with the re - organization.  (2)   Firing at fleeing enemy vehicles/ personnel after attack .  (3)   Can be employed ahead of the reorg positions and beat back enemy  Counter attack efforts and also direct indirect fire (Artillery or Mortar).  8.   Advance  a.   To deny enemy access to key terrain through controlled precision fires, denying  enemy advantage.  b.   To cho ose positions for own force.  c.   To gather information about the enemy.  9.   Withdrawal  a.   Delay the enemy by inflicting casualties.  b.   Observe avenues of approach.  c.   Cover key obstacles with precision fire.  d.   Direct artillery fire against large enemy formations.  10.   Minor   Operations  a.   Ambush   / Raid  (1)   Cover the move to target area.  (2)   Provide long range observation/ surveillance support to force.  (3)   Engage the fleeing enemy or other opportunity targets.  (4)   Prevent reinforcement of the enemy forces.  (5)   Precise engagement of sentries / k ey personnel.  (6)   Engage the Ieading vehicle for effective road bock, when required.  (7)   Cover the withdrawal of the force after completion of the operation.

--- PAGE 165 ---
165  RESTD  RESTD  a.   Patrols .   Mission of patrolling will also dictate the employment of sniper  weapon. It can be employed both   in recconnaisance and fighting patrol to provide  observation,   fire   suport   and   to   counter   enemy   snipping.   However,   the  employment in patrols has to be carefully weighed against the mobility of the  patrol and chances of it getting into the hands of enemy. L SR or SSR are more  suited for patrolling operations as the HSR becomes inconvenient to move from  one place to the other due to its size and weight.  11.   Sub Conventional Operations  a.   Defensive Role  (1)   Security  (a)   Assist in sealing off borders by countering infiltrai on / illegal  border crossing.  (b)   Protection of: -  i.   Lines of Communication / Road Opening Day.  ii.   Base camp.  iii.   Important venues / VIPs.  (c)   Employment at important / dominating piquets.  (d)   Camp security.  (2)   Employment at Post  (a)   Strengthen defence of important posts.  (b)   Emp loyed   at   vantage   points   for   information   gathering   /  surveillance.  (c)   Give   early   warning   about   development   of   situation   in  neighbouring posts.  (d)   Provide long range observation.  (e)   Counter   sniping   to   include location,   identifying   and   engaging  hostile snipers.  b.   Offen sive Role  (1)   Raid / Assaullt on Terrorists’ Stronghold  (a)   Provide covering fire to assaulting troops by engaging Terrorists  through windows, loopholes and other openings in the stronghold.  (b)   Prevent escape of Terrorists.  (c)   Provide intimate protection to own troo ps during entry and exit.  (d)   Assistt in cordon of stronghold.  (e)   Counter sniping by locating and engaging the Terrorist snipers.  (f)   Observation and commentary/ reporting; passage of info.  (2)   Ambushing the Terrorists  (a)   Observation of ambush site using optical devic es.  (b)   Give early warning about the target approaching.  (c)   Act as road block party and stop the Ieading vehicle by engaging  the vehicle or the driver.  (d)   Stop miscreants’ reinforcement.  (e)   Engage fleeing enemy from ambush site.  12.   Counter Sniper Role .   When an ene my sniper threat has been identified in the  sniper team's Area of Operation, the team is employed to eliminate the enemy sniper. During a  counter sniper operation, the team must ignore battle activity and home on the enemy sniper.

--- PAGE 166 ---
166  RESTD  RESTD  When an enemy sniper is o perating in a unit's area, the sniper team ensures the unit employs  passive counter measures to defend against enemy sniper fire which incl:  a.   Do not establish routines such as consistent meal times, ammunition resupply or  day to day activities that have dev eloped into a routine  b.   Conduct all meetings, briefings or gatherings of personnel under cover or during  limited visibility.  c.   Do not display ranks on uniforms. Do not salute officers. Leaders should not use  authoritative mannerisms.  d.   Do not display awareness o f the presence of enemy sniper at any time.  13.   Conclusion .   The accuracy and effective long range capability of sniper rifle can only  be utilized to the optimum if the snipers are rigorously trained and have carried out extensive  sequential firing practic es in different weather environments. Employment of the sniper teams  also requires consideration and can pay rich dividends in all operations of war if the weaponn is  utilized to its true potential.

After firing the burned propellant produces a huge amount  of gases, which on one hand pushes the bullet fwd, on the other hand acts  on the bolt assy through the case base. But the bolt assy cannot   move  backward to open the chamber bec the bolt locks are still in locking  recesses. When the bullet passes over the gas port a portion of the gases  after passing through the gas port and the gas regulator pushes the piston  rod and the bolt frame backward.   At this instant before the bullet leaves  the barrel, the bolt assy locks the chamber. As the bolt frame mov  backward, the firing pin extension also mov backward and the camming  shoulder of firing pin extension leaves the bolt locks, the bolt locks are  clo sed by the func of the shaped groove lvls. The bolt and bolt frame mov  further back thus achieving unlocking. At the same time the return spring  is gradually pressed under the action of piston.  (2)   Extracting .   When the bolt frame fol the bolt slide backward e xtractor  extracts the Cart from the chamber.  (3)   Ejecting .   During the rearward mov of the bolt frame, bolt slide and  bolt when the ejector hits against the lvl surface of the receiver, its end is  driven out, ejecting the empty case out of the chamber.  (4)   Feedin g  (a)   Feeding ‘A’ .   Insert the linked belt with ammo into the feed mech  through the belt holding pawl and make the first rd held by the  belt holding pawl.  (b)   Feeding ‘B’ .   In the process of rearward mov of the bolt frame the  cocking knob bring the long feed arm b ackward which, through its  axle and the short feed arm, forces the feed slide to the lt and feed  pawl mov the next rd to feed slot for feeding and when the bolt  assy mov fwd the cocking knob bring the long feed arm fwd and  through the action of its axle, t he short feed arm moves the feed  slide and feed   pawl rt to catch the next Cart.  (5)   Cocking .   During the rearward mov of the bolt as the bolt slide passes  over the sear, the sear spring quickly stretches to force the sear up. Thus,  the sear groove is locked. Wh en the bolt slide moves fwd the slide lug  engages with sear notch, and the bol t slide comes in cocked posn.

--- PAGE 171 ---
171  RESTD  RESTD  b.   Fwd Mov of the Bolt  (1)   Chambering .   When the gnr pulls the trigger, the trigger bar raises the  sear bar up, and lowers down the sear until it is out   of the sear groove. At  this mov the bolt slide quickly moves fwd under the force of the return  spring. When the bolt body passes by the feed slot, the bolt head push a  Cart into the chamber and when the body reaches its posn the extractor  catches the rim o f the case.  (2)   Locking   After completion of chambering the firing pin extension  contits fwd mov and the camming shoulders push the bolt locks apart into  the bolt locking recesses and it comes in locking posn.  (3)   Firing .   When the gnr pulls the trigger the trigg er bar rises the sear bar up,  at this moment the bolt slide moves fwd under the force of returning  spring bringing along the firing pin extension. Its head strikes the firing  pin and the firing pin in turn strikes the primer, thus firing the bullet.  2.   Stopp ages and Remedies  a. 173  RESTD  RESTD  6x bul lets in insp ring   MPI in the white patch  ZEROING, BORE SIGHTING AND ADJUSTING THE SIGHTS OF 12.7 MM AA MG  GSP - 1747 and Tech Manual  1.   Zeroing .   Testing the sights of wpn and if nec to carry out adjustment, so that  whenever wpn is fired with correct aim, the bulle ts hit the aiming pt.  2.   Occasion for Zeroing 12.7 mm AA MG  a.   On receipt from ord.  b.   Before firing.  c.   After changing of maj parts.  d.   Before going to battle fd.  e.   After station changing.  f.   On crew changing.  g.   After original barrel changing.  3.   Conditions Req for Zeroing  a.   Vis .   Tgt is vis and wind is in normal condition.  b.   Rg .   100 ms.  c.   Tgt   4’x4’ with spec marking of insp ring and  with 10x8   cm white patch in centre.  d.   Firer .   A good firer should do this prac.  e.   Sight .   The rg on rear sight should be 100 ms.  f.   Wpn .   The wpn should b e cleaned and insp.  4.   Principles of Zeroing  a.   For front sight mov towards the error.  b.   For rear sight mov against the error.  c.   Fire a check gp.  5.   Method .   Fire 8   x   rd   gp single shot  with same aim if 6   x bullet s   hit in any of the insp  ring and the MPI is in the wh ite patch or close to  the white patch, the gun is zeroed. If there is a  difference, find out MPI to measure the actual  error, and carry out nec adjustment.  6.   Method of Adjustment  a.   Horizontal Adjustment .   There is an index line  marked on front sight pro tector and on front sight seat,  which indicates zero posn of front sight. For horizontal  mov there are six indication lines marked rt and lt of  indicator. The space between two indication lines is 1  mm which shows 9 cm difference on a tgt placed at 100  ms.   We   can   use   5   indication   lines   rt   and   lt   for  correction. It means 45 cm correction is allowed rt and  lt on the front sight.

--- PAGE 174 ---
174  RESTD  RESTD  b.   Vertical Adjustment .   There   are   21   rings   or  thread marked on front sight pin and a ring is equal to 1  mm on pin which shows 7 mm on   a tgt placed at 100 ms.  We can use 10 rotations (ring) upward and 10 rotations  downward. It means we can adjust 70 cm error up and  down. For mov   the pin upward, move it anti clockwise  and for mov down, move the pin clockwise. Als o apply  front sight princi ple.  7.   Bore Sighting  a.   Definition .   To align the line of sight and line of bore on a specified pt is  defined as bore sighting.  b.   Advantages of Bore Sighting  (1)   Saves time.  (2)   Saves ammo.  (3)   Can be done any time and anywhere.  (4)   There is no need of long rg.  (5)   Accurate z eroing can be achieved.  c.   Prep for Bore Sighting  (1)   Mt the gun.  (2)   Place the specified tgt on the specified dist.  (3)   Use a testing mirror or cross on muzzle.  (4)   3 Nos will carry it.  d.   Method of Bore Sighting  (1)   Testing tgt method.  (2)   Dist tgt method.  (3)   Testing Tgt Method  (a)   Place testing tgt on 25 ms dist from muzzle end.  (b)   Prep the gun, place testing mirror and make a bore sight cross.  (c)   Place rear sight slide on zero posn.  (d)   Ensure that the front sight indicator is on zero posn.  (e)   Align the bore on specified cross on the tgt.  (f)   Now align the front sight by moving rt, lt, up and down on specified  cross on the tgt.  (g)   Then align the front AA sight central aiming hole on the tgt by  moving rear AA sight.  (h)   The process ensures that the airplane model is in 12 ‘O’ clock  (i)   dir.  (4)   Dist Tgt   Method  (a)   Mt the gun.  (b)   Place testing mirror or cross on muzzle.  (c)   Place the AA sight on gun.  (d)   Place rear sight slide on 900 rg scale.  (e)   Select an aiming pt between 1000 and 2000Ms.  (f)   First align bore sight cross and lock all the mech.  (g)   Secondly align front sigh t on the subj aiming pt.

178  RESTD  RESTD  and the   pt er. The graduation near es t to the two pts where the seen   tgt meets the  frame and the   pt er respecti vely, shows the rg of the tgt.  4.   Use of AA Sight  a.   Correct Determination of Data .   The correct determination of data is an  imp factor for correct determination of the lead qty .  (1)   How to Determine Tgt Speed .   The tgt speed is usually determined  by the gun no 1 according to his cbt experience. In order to achieve  accuracy, he should keep in mi nd the cruising speed of en ac.  (2)   To Determine Tgt Foreshortening .   The proportion of vis ual length  to the actual length of an ac is called foreshortening .   It can be assessed by  dividing the full length of the plane fuselage into four parts. For example,  the actual length of the fuselage is 20 ms and the visual length is 10 ms. Its  foreshorten ing   is   10/20   =   2/4.   If   the   visual   length   is   5   ms   its  foreshortening will be 5/20=1/4.  (3)   Correct Setting of Tgt Course .   No 2 rotates the small handle on  the front sight to keep the course line parallel to the tgt course line and  the ac model in the d ir of the tgt course. Thus, the dir of lead qty is  determined. During firing, bec the tgt is gradually apch, no. 2 should  continuously and gently rotate the small handle of the course sight to keep  the course line parallel to the tgt course line and the ac   model in the dir of  the tgt course to ensure that the lead pt always comes across the tgt  course. When the tgt is dirapch, the course lines are in vertical posn and  the ac model is upward. When the tgt is dirreceding, the course lines are  also in vertical   posn but the ac model downward.  (4)   Correct sel of aiming hole .   The value of lead qty ac sight is rep by the no  of aiming holes. The aiming hole to be used is decided by No1 according to  the tgt speed,   dist ( rg) and its foreshortening. It can be sel with the   aid of  the aiming hole table given below.  AIMING HOLES TABLE  Tgt Speed   Rg (ms)  1800 - 1200   1200 - 600   600 - 200  Foreshortening  kmph   m/sec   1/4   2/4   ¼   2/4   1/4   2/4  Aiming Holes  300   80   2   4   2   3   1   2  400   110   3   5   2   4   2   3  500   140   3   6   3   5   2   4  600   170   4   7   3   6   3   5  700   200   4   8   4   7   3   6  800   220   5   9   4   8   4   7  900   250   5   10   5   9   4   8  1000   280   6   11   5   10   5   9  Example .   For example, the tgt speed is 700 km/hr, the rg is 1000 ms and the foreshortening  is 2/4, so the aiming hole sel from the table is ‘7’. Now, for the moder n fast flying ac it will be

--- PAGE 179 ---
179  RESTD  RESTD  difficult for No 1 to consult the table time and again, and firing will be delayed. A brief method is  adopted. If the rg of the ac is between 600 - 1200 ms and foreshortening is 2/4 then the first no of  the three figures of the ac   speed in km/hr in the column is the no of the aiming hole to be used.  When the tgt rg is greater than 1200 ms, one more aiming hole should be added, when the tgt rg  is less than 600 ms one hole should be subtracted from the number. When tgt foreshortening   is  1/4, half of the original no is subtracted. When tgt foreshortening is 3/4, half of the original no  should be added and when the tgt foreshortening is 4/4, the original no should be doubled. Gun Drill  3.   To Form Det Rear .   The det will fall in two   ranks with one pace between thr r anks:   -  a.   1, 3 and 4 in the front rank.  b.   2 in the rear rank.  c.   1 is not c overed and will fall in one pace from the shoulder stock of the MG.  d.   Remaining nos stand to his lt, shoulder to shoulder.  4.   To Tell Off .   On the order ‘Tell Off’ the rt hand man of the frontrank numbers himself 1.  The rt hand man of the rear rank numbers him self 2. His front - rank man 3 and the last man of  the front - rank numbers himself 4.  5.   To Change Rd .   On the order ‘change rd’ no 1 takes a pace to the rear, a short  pace to the lt and then a pace to the front. At the same time the remainder of the fron t rank takes  two short paces to the rt.  6. No 1 order “Action in AA Role”.  b.   2, 3 and 4 lift the gun upwards from the rear, lt and rt respectively.  c.   1 unlocks the front locking handle, presses the locking plunger, takes the fro nt leg  to fully fwdposn and again locks the locking handle. He unlocks the leg fixing  handle, adjusts the leg in action and the locks the fixing handle.  d.   2 closes the elbow resting saddle and gets ready to place the gun upside down  with his hand on the gri pping handles.  e.   3 stands on the lt and 4 on the rt leg.  f.   1 orders “lift” and 2 lifts the gun.

(9)   1 unlocks the leg fixing handle, adjusts th e leg for gr role, again locks the  leg fixing handle and places the leg on the gr.  (10)   2 adjust the elbow resting saddle. Note:   This gun should always be cocked with safety con latch at ‘fire’. 2 should  ensure that at the time of cocking, the trigger is not pressed.  (5)   3 ensures that the belt slides in when   the loading handle is pulled.  (6)   2 sets the safety lock con latch to ‘safe’ and reports ‘safe’.

EMP OF 12. 7 MM AA MG IN   MOPS, DEF, ATTK AND SCW  GSP   –   1747  Emp of 12.7 mm AA   MG in all Ops of war  1.   Conventional Ops .   Keeping in view the chars   of 12.7 mm AA MG, its utility in  conventional ops is as fol: -  a.   Attk  (1)   Protection of Assy A and FUP.  (2)   In initial fire plan.  (3)   Pr otection of flks of own tps against en   armr.  (4)   In BOF.  (5)   Provide fire sp by tgt   en bkrs.  (6)   Loc ahead of reorg posn.  (7)   Form part of THPs.  b.   Pri of Engaging Tgts   during Attk .   During any stage of attk, the pri of engaging  tgts should be as fol:   -  (1)   Arm r, sty or mov.  (2)   Hy wpns.  (3)   Pill boxes, bkrs and concrete emplacements.  c.   Def  (1)   With Protective Dets .   12.7 mm AA MG may be used to provide  local and short rg A tk protection to the tps   depl as protective dets or   to  provide mutual sp to the BSWS   asg to the prot ective dets.  (2)   In Main Def Posn .   The accuracy and   lethality   of this wpn makes it a  deadly wpn against attk   en tps .  2.   SCW .   12.7 mm AA MG is not only useful in conventional ops but its emp in SCW ops   also  can prove to be a force multiplier. In order to accrue   max benefits of its char s , the optimum  utilization of 12.7 mm AA MG in fol SCW roles is very imp: -

--- PAGE 184 ---
184  RESTD  RESTD  a.   Emp as Force Multiplier .   In certain circumstances where the fire sp of own  arty or mors is not aval or possible due to dist or terrain, the aval 12.7 mm AA   MG  can be grouped to provide a dir, aimed, accurate and conc fire onto the tgt.  b.   Rd Block .   In certain cases where an ambush is to be laid against   Ts   mov in a  vehcny, the 12.7 mm AA MG fire can be used as an eff rd block in a similar fashion  as in   conventional ambushes.  c.   C&S   Op .   During such op, the wpn can be used to eff engage any   Ts  hideouts and to create entry pts in walls of compounds/houses where entry is  otherwise difficult/impossible. Moreover, any fleeing or rft   vehs of   Ts   in the are a  can also be engaged eff.  d.   CP s (Static and Mob) .   Whether the wpn is emp in a static or mob CP, the  eff of the wpn   can not be denied. Once the sentry has spotted a veh mov towards  the CP with aggressive and nefarious designs, it can be easily stopped/engag ed at  a safe dist before it can blow itself or strike the CP. This way a substantial amount  of damage and loss of lives / eqpt can be avoided.

--- PAGE 186 ---
186  RESTD  RESTD  JUGDING DIST (JD) & USE OF AIDS TO JUDGING DIST  Aim  1.   To teach how to judge dist and use of aids to   judging dist.  Intro .  2.   JD   forms the basis to achieve the max eff of firearms. All wpns in gen and SA in particular  have a ltd rg. Certain wpns have longer rg whereas others have a short rg. The fire eff of all wpns  is completely nullified if the dists a re judged inaccurately. Although there are certain devices  and means by which we can judge and measure the dists very accurately and that include air  photos, rg finder and so on, yet these facilities are neither aval nor desirable at sec lvl where the  wpn   in use has a very ltd rg.   So, in order to help us in doing our task of JD accurately certain  methods and aids have been devised. With prac and keen eye for the gr, it is a very easy task.  TYPES OF METHOD  3.   There are fol two types of method:   -  a.   Unit Measu re Method  b.   Appearance Method  Unit Measure Method  4.   Take 100 meters (109 yards) as your unit and see how many units can fit in between  yourself and the object.  5.   This   method is not useful unless   you can see all the gr between yourself and objective,  nor for   dists over 400 meters (437 yards).  Appearance Method  6.   Another method to judge dist to an obj is to study what it looks like compared with its  surroundings.   These are: -  a.   At 200 Yds .   All parts of body are distinct.  b.   At 250 Yds .   Blade of foresight covers a knee ling man.  c.   At 300 Yds .   Out line of the face becomes blurred.  d.   At 400 Yds .   Out line of the body remains but face is difficult.to  distinguish. Blade of foresight covers a man standing.  e.   At 500 Yds .   Body appears to be larger slightly from shoulders. Move of limb s  can still be seen (Show the men at all these ranges and discuss with the students).  f.   At 600 Yds .   Head appears a dot details not visible.  7.   Conditions that Affect Appearance  a.   Things seem closer than they are, when:   -  (1)   The light is bright or the sun is shinin g from behind you.  (2)   They are bigger than other things around them.  (3)   There is dead gr between them and you.  (4)   They are higher up than you.  b.   Things seem farther away than they are, when:   -  (1)   The light is bad or the sun is in your eyes.  (2)   They are   smaller to   othe r things around them.  (3)   You are looking across a valley or down a street or a ride in a wood  (4)   You are lying down.  AIDS TO JUDGING DIST  8.   Halving .   Choose a pt that you think is half way to your tgt, estimate the dist to that  pt and double it.

--- PAGE 187 ---
187  RESTD  RESTD  9.   Bracketing .   S ay to yourself that ‘the tgt could not be more than x meters, nor less than  y meters away, add x and y and halve the result. The answer is the rg. For instance, if x is 1000  and y is 600, the rg is 800. The farther the tgt is away, the wider should be your   bracket.  10.   Key Rgs ,   If you know the rg to any pt in your arc, you can estimate the dist to other  objects from it.  11.   Unit Average .   Get several men to judge a dist and to take the average of their  answers.  RECOGNITION AND INDICATION OF TGT  METHOD OF INDIC ATION  1.   There are fol two types of method:   -  a.   Direct Method  b.   Indirect Method  2.   Before going any few terms which are going to be used frequently will be defined:   -  a.   Gen Dir . It is an imaginary line passing through the centre of the en.  b.   Ref Pt .   An obj wh ich can be used as a ref in future.  Direct Method  3.   Obvious tgts are indicated by what is known as the direct method.  4.   The section comd tells you the rg and where to look and describes the tgt, the terms he  uses are: -  a.   “Axis of the arc”, for tgts on or ver y near the axis.  b.   “Left” or “Right”, for the tgts 90 degrees from the axis.  c.   “Slightly”, “quarter”, “half” or “three quarter” and “left” or “right” for tgts b/w the  axis and “left” or “right”.  d.   An example might be “300   -   half   –   right   –   lone bush”.  Indirec t Method  5.   Difficult tgts are indicated by this method. The section comd may use direct method in  conjunction with reference pts, reference pts and clock ray or all above three ways of indicating  a tgt as explained below.  a.   Reference Pts  (1)   For less obvious t gts, the section comd may use reference pts and the  direct method together and perhaps “above” and “below” as well.  (2)   An example is, “300   –   bushy topped tree (the reference pt)   –   slightly right  –   small bush (the tgt)  b.   Clock Ray  (1)   For more difficult tgts, th e section comd may use a reference pt or clock  ray.  (2)   Imagine a clock face standing up on the landscape with its centre on the  reference pt.  (3)   To indicate a tgt, the section comd tells you the rg, the reference pt and  whether tgt is to right or left of it and   the appropriate hour on the clock  face, to give you the direction to look in from the reference pt.  (4)   An example is, “300   –   tree   –   right   –   4 o’ clock   –   small bush”  c.   Use of Degrees .   For extremely difficult tgts, degrees may be used along  with clock r ay method.

--- PAGE 188 ---
188  RESTD  RESTD  d.   Degrees are measured from the reference pt to the tgt. However, if the tgt to be  indicated is 6 o’clock or 12 o’clock of the reference pt, degrees may not be used.  (1)   An example is, “300   –   tree (reference pt) -   right 4 o’clock   –   5 degrees   –  small   bush”.  MOVING / CRAWLING   WITH AND WITHOUT RIF  1.   Method of Mov with Rif  a.   The Monkey Run  (1)   It is simply crawling on hands and knees.  (2)   It is useful behind cover above two feet high.  (3)   You can go quite fast, but the faster you go, the more noise you make.  (4)   If you reall y want to be quiet, always choose a safe place (with no twigs to  crack, etc) to put your hands and then put your knees exactly where your  hands  have been.  (5)   Keep your backside and head down, but observe as you go.  (6)   If you have a rif, hold it at the pt of bal ance with your left hand and see  that no dirt gets into the muzzle.  b.   The Leopard Crawl  (1)   It is crawling on elbows and the inside of the knees.  (2)   It is useful behind very low cover.  (3)   Propel yourself along by alternate elbows and knees and roll your body a  litt le as you bend each knee; or let one leg trail behind, and use only one  knee. Keep your heels, head, body and elbows down, but observe as you  go.  (4)   If you have a rif, make it safe and then hold it either with your right hand  on the small of the butt and your   left hand at the pt of balance, with the  magazine forward, or by the front of the sling with your right hand, with  the rif rested on your right arm, and the muzzle forwards.  The Monkey Run with and without Rif

--- PAGE 189 ---
189  RESTD  RESTD  c.   The Roll  (1)   It is often the quickest way of getting away from a spot, such as a c rest  line, where the en has seen you.  (2)   Roll with your arms and your rif, if you have one, close into your side, but  you must ensure that your rif has been made safe.  d.   The Walk  (1)   Hold your rif in a posn that you can use it instantly. Remain   alert, keep  your head up and observe as you proceed. A   rg card  aids in planning and con fires and aids the wpn crews in acquiring tgts during pds of ltd  visibility. On the blank card, the five equidistant circles have no rgs marked on them; use them for  any series of rg.  4.   A section normally has an arc of fire; but you must record rgs in every direction, so that  you   are really prepared to conduct all - rd def; hence the design of the card.  5. Decide on the objects that you want to record the rg to; include positions that the  enemy may occupy and obstacles, gaps in hedgerows, etc, near which he is likely  to pass.  e.   Set th e card by the setting rays and draw in rays in the direction of all the other  objects.  f.   Against the position of each object, print a short description of how it looks to the  naked eye, and the rg to it.

193  RESTD  RESTD  INTRO TO GPS AND GARMIN 12 XL  Garmin 12 XL and ( www.wikipedia.com )  1.   Intro .   The   Global   Posn ing System   ( GPS ) is a space - based global nav satellite sys  (G NSS) that provides loc and time info in all weather, anywhere on or near the Earth, where  there is an unobstructed line of sight to   four or more GPS satellites . It is maintained by the  United States govt and is freely accessible by anyone with a GPS receiv er.  2.   The GPS project was   dev in 1973   to overcome the ltds of previous nav sys, integrating  ideas from several predecessors, incl a number of classified engineering design studies from the  1960s. GPS was created and realized by the U.S. Department of Defens e (USDOD) and was  originally run with 24 satellites.   It became fully op in 1994 .  3.   In addn to GPS, other sys are in use or under dev. The   Russian Global Nav Satellite Sys  (GLONASS)   was in use by only the Russian mil, until it was made fully avail to civs in   2007.  There are also the planned   Chinese Compass nav   sys and the   European Union's Galileo  posn ing sys.  4.   Components of GPS .   The current GPS consists of three maj segments.  a.   Space Segments .   The GPS space  segment c onsists of 24 satellites ,  this   incl   thre e   spares   also.   The  satellites   have   been   placed   in   six  orbital   planes   with   three   or   four  satellites   in   each   plane.   Only four  satellites are normally req for nav  purpose. If more are visible above 15  deg on the horizon then four offering  best   geometry   are   se l   auto   by  receivers. Each orbital plane has an  inclination of 55 deg to the equator  at the orbital ht of about 20,000 kms.  Each satellite completes an orbit in approx 12 hrs.  b.   Con Segment .   T he   con  segment comprises of one  master con sta (MCS) at  Colorad o USA   and Monitor  Sta   at   Hawaii ,   Diego  Garcia,   Kwajalien   and  Ascension   Island.   The  monitor   stas   passively  track all GPS satellites in  view   collecting   rg   data  from   each.   This   info   is  passed to the MCS, where  satellite   ephemeris  parameter   is   est   and  predicte d.  c.   User Segment .   The user segment consists of variety of mil and civ GPS  receivers specifically designed to receive, decode and process GPS sig.

--- PAGE 194 ---
194  RESTD  RESTD  5.   Concept of GPS Working .   A GPS receiver calc its posn by precisely timing the signals  sent by GPS satellit es high above the Earth. Each satellite continually transmits   msg   that include:  a.   The time the message was transmitted.  b.   Precise orbital info (the ephemeris).  c.   The   gen   sys health and rough orbits of all GPS satellites (the almanac). The  receiver uses the   ms g   it receives to determine the transit time of each message  and computes the dist to each satellite. These dist along with the satellites' loc are  used with the possible aid of   trilateration , to compute the posn of the receiver.  Three satellites might seem   enough to solve for posn since space has three  dimensions and a posn near the Earth's surface can be assumed. However, even a  very small clock error multiplied by the very large speed of lt —   the speed at  which satellite signals propagate   —   results in a la rge   posn al error. Therefore,  receivers use four or more satellites to solve for the receiver's loc and time.  6.   Emphasis Data Geometry .   The better the geometry the more precise and used in  obtaining exact posn of each satellite. Each satellite transmits two   codes. P code (precise or  projected code) and C/A code (clear acquisition or con accuracy code). P code is reserved for use  by US Department of Def, C/A code has unltd access and is aval to every GPS user.  7.   Accuracy .   The most remarkable feature of the GPS   is its accuracy. Posn accuracy in  cms is obtained with sel aval. However, with C/A code the accuracy dec to 30 ms or better  depending upon type of receiver. The accuracy of hand held GPS receiver is normally + -   100 ms .  8.   Interference .   Another spec feat ure of GPS receiver is its resistance to interference  from condns that disturb other elec nav sys. It incl thunder, lt, radio sigs, on board and on shore  elec instls and portable radio receivers.  9.   Handheld GPS Receivers .   These are common GPS receivers with   accuracy lvl of + -  100. These receivers normally have five to twelve channels working simultaneously to collect  loc and compute data from satellites. Data received is rapidly processed to compute current loc,  elev, vel / speed every sec.  10.   Uses  a.   Mil  (1)   Nav.  (2)   Tgt tracking.  (3)   Msl and proj guidance.  (4)   Search and rescue.  (5)   Reccee.  b.   Civ  (1)   Disaster relief mgmt svcs.  (2)   Geofencing: Veh tracking sys, person tracking sys, and pet tracking.  (3)   Geotagging:   Applying   location   coordinates   to   digital   objects   such   as  photographs and   other docus for purposes such as creating map overlays.  (4)   GPS ac tracking.  (5)   GPS tours.  (6)   Map making.  (7)   Nav.  (8)   Surveying.  (9)   Tectonics. GPS enables direct fault motion measurement in earthquakes.

--- PAGE 195 ---
195  RESTD  RESTD  11.   Other Sys .   Other satellite nav sys in use or various states of de v incl:   -  a.   Galileo .   A global sys being dev by the European Union and other partner  ctys.  b.   Beidou .   People's Republic of China's regional sys, covering Asia and  the West Pacific.  c.   COMPASS .   People's Republic of China's global sys, planned to be op by 2020.  d.   GLONASS .   Russia's global nav sys.  e.   IRNSS .   India's regional nav sys, covering India and Northern Indian Ocean.  f.   QZSS .   Japanese regional sys cove ring Asia and Oceania.  12.   How to Use GPS in Pakistan .   The GPS after being issued from Ord or bought  commercially needs to be cal for use in Pakistan. Fol ste ps are to be taken for cal .  a. (2)   India Zone IIA (INDIA IIA) for usage on Grid 2 maps.  c.   Set Map Datum to WGS 84. ( World Geodetic Sys 1984 )  d.   Grid zone is written at the bottom of every map sheet in Red Color that indicates  the l oc of grid. Anx A.

The GPS 12XL is a   powerful nav tool that can guide you anywhere  in the world. To better understand its op and cap, it may be helpful to  review the basic terms and concepts briefly explained below. Other nav  and GPS definitions used in the manual are defined in the appropria te ref  sections of the manual.  a.   Almanac Data .   Satellite constellation info (incl loc  and health of satellites) that is transmitted to your receiver  from every GPS satellite. Almanac data must be acquired  before GPS nav can begin.  b.   Bearing .   The   compas s   dir   from   your   posn   to   a  destination. Course Made Good (CMG) The bg from the  “active from” posn (your starting pt) to your present posn.  c.   Crosstrack Error (XTK) .   The dist you are off a desired  course in either dir. Desired Track (DTK) The compass  course b etween the “from” and “to” way pts. Differential  GPS (DGPS) An extn of the GPS sys that uses land - based  radio beacons to transmit posn corrns to GPS receivers.  d.   Est Time of Arr (ETA) .   The time of day of your arr  at a destination. Est Time Enroute (ETE ). The time left to your destination at your  present speed.  e.   Grid .   Coord sys that proj the earth on a flat surface, using sq zones for posn  measurements. UTM/UPS and Maiden head formats are grid sys.  f.   Gd Speed .   The   vel   you are traveling relative to a gd   posn.  Anx A

--- PAGE 197 ---
197  RESTD  RESTD  g.   Longitude .   An east / west measurement of  posn   in   relation   to   the   Prime   Meridian,   an  imaginary circle that passes through the north  and south poles.  h.   Nav .   The process of traveling from one place to  another and knowing where you are in relation  to yo ur desired course.  i.   Posn .   An exact, unique loc based on a geo coord  sys.  j.   Track (TRK) .   The dir of mov relative to a gd  posn.  k.   Universal Transverse Mercator (UTM) .   A  grid coord sys that proj global sections onto a flat  surface to measure posn in specific   zones.  l.   Vel   Made Good (VMG) .   The speed you  are traveling in the dir of the destination.  m.   Way pt .   A   specific   loc   saved   in   the  receiver’s memory.  n.   2D Nav .   At least three satellites with good  geometry   have   been   locked   onto   and   a   2 -  dimensional posn fix   (latitude and longitude) is being calc. ‘2D Diff’ will appear  when you are receiving DGPS corrns in 2D mode.  o.   3D Nav .   At least four satellites with good geometry have been locked  onto, and your posn is now being calc in latitude, longitude and alti tude. ‘3D Diff’  will appear when you are receiving DGPS corrns in 3D mode.  p.   EPE .   Est posn error appears on the satellite page while acquiring data from  satellites.  2.   Pages of Garmin 12XL  Satellite Page.  3.   The Satellite Page shows satellite posns and s igs strength.  Satellite posns are displayed using two circles and a center pt. The  outer circle shows satellites on level with the horizon; the inner circle  is 45º above the horizon; and the center   pt   reps satellites dir overhead.  Knowing satellite posns w ill show you the dir of any blocked sigs. The  bottom of the page contains a row of sigs strength bars corresponding  to each satellite being used.  Posn Page .  4.   The Posn Page shows you where you are, what dir you are hdg  and how fast you are going. The top o f the page contains a compass  tape which is a graphic rep of your hdg. Your track and   speed are

--- PAGE 198 ---
198  RESTD  RESTD  indicated imed below. The rest of the page shows your current posn in three dimensions:  latitude, longitude, and altitude. A trip odometer and 12/24 - hour clock   are also provided.  Map Page.  5.   The Map Page acts as a window. It allows you to view your posn,  the “path” you have traveled over, and nearby way pts. A diamond icon  in the center of the screen reps your current posn. As you move, you will  see a thin line   -   called a track log   -   appear along the path you have just  covered. Names of stored way pts can also be shown on the map. The  bottom corners of the map will always display your current track and  speed. When going to a way -   pt, highlt an on - screen way pt, o r using the  panning tgt crosshair, the corresponding dis and bearing are shown at  the top corners of the map. The top of the screen contains the zoom, pan,  and con -   figuration fields.  Nav Page.  6.   A nav page gives you steering guidance when going to a way p t.  The GPS 12XL has two nav page choices:   The   Compass Page and the  Highway Page. The Compass Page is the default and will be briefly  explained here. The Highway Page is covered on pages 35 and 38. The  Compass Page shows the destination way -   pt   at the top o f the page with  the bearing (BRG) and dist (DST) to the way pt shown below. The center  of the page contains a compass ring and arrow to show you the dir of the  way pt from the dir you are mov. The bottom of the page gives your  track (TRK), speed (SPD), and   a sel field for est time to arrival (ETA), est  time enroute (ETE), course to steer (CTS), cross track error, (XTK),   vel  made good (VMG), or turn bg (TRN).  Menu Page.  7.   The last primary page is the Menu Page. The Menu Page gives  you access to the GPS 12XL ’s way pt mgmt, route, track log, and setup  features through a list of sub menus. Wpn Handling refers to regulations regarding actions req to handle   the indl’s wpn  during peace and war. It is the practice of using, transporting, storing and disposing of firearms/  wpns and ammo. The INSAS LMG is based on the Soviet   RPK   , but has a number of features f rom other  successful designs, such as the   FN FAL   (gas sys),   HK33   (charging handle),   Steyr AUG   (magazine),  and Galil (buttstock). The Inf wpns and SAs have taken centuries in dev to the present lvl. Every dev which  alters the cap si gnificantly also revolutionizes the warfare. In past two decades; a wide rg of dev  taking place both in SAs and ammo. The aim being making these wpns compact, easy to handle,  accurate and more lethel. Some ctys are more inclined towards reducing cal, wt, s ize and recoil  of the wpns while others relied on dev wpns on family sys. Latest Trends in AP Bullets .   There   are   many   new  inventions in AP ammo. Some of them are as under:   -  a.   Tungsten Carbide Rds .   Tungsten Carbid e is very hy  and lethal rd. Solid tungsten carbide bullets are very hard  and   eff.   Tungsten   Carbide   is   a   chemical   compound  containing equal parts of tungsten and carbon items. In  its most basic form, Tungston carbide is a fine grey  powder. It is two times s tiffer than steel and is double the  density of steel; nearly midway b/w that of lead and gold.  b.   Titanium Rds .   The bullet is a solid titanium with a Teflon jacket. It is almost  half the wt of steel, yet has same str. The bullet travels faster than   an armr -  piercing rd and slows down quicker in the tgt. It can piecrearmr only at pt blank  to med rg.  c.   Depleated Uranium .   Depleated uranium is created as  by   product   of   the   processes   used   convert   natural  uranium for use as nuclear fuel for nuclear   wpns. Unlike  other piercing rds the core is made up of depleated  uranium.
